Modal Fallacies in Syllogisms
Author explains how the modality of the minor premise determines the modality of the conclusion, using a medical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the modality of a syllogistic conclusion based on the minor premise and identify modal fallacies.

Core Idea
The conclusion's necessity depends on the minor premise's necessity; ignoring modality leads to false claims of certainty.

The author presents a syllogism about disease and health, then clarifies that the conclusion's modality must match the minor premise's modality. If the minor premise is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if contingent, the conclusion is contingent; if absolute, absolute. This is a critical lesson for avoiding overgeneralisation in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit syllogism in the passage
Find the syllogism: 'the conclusion is not in any disease, and disease is in every human'. This is a categorical syllogism with terms: health, disease, human.
2
Clarify the modality of the minor premise
The minor premise is 'disease is in every human'. Determine its modality: is it necessary, contingent, or absolute? The author says it can be any of these.
3
Apply the rule that the conclusion's modality follows the minor premise
If the minor is necessary, the conclusion 'health is not in any human' is necessary; if contingent, the conclusion is contingent; if absolute, absolute.
4
Identify the fallacy: assuming necessity without checking
The error occurs when one thinks the conclusion is necessarily true without considering the modality of the minor premise. This is a modal fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون ذلك الطائر بعينه وإن كانت الشمعة تلك الشمعة. ولا يبعد أن يكون الشيء يغاير ذاته في أحواله والمغايرة لا يصح مع وحدة بالعدد. ثمَّ الأوسط إذا يعني ليس بشخصي فمن حق الكبرى أن تكون صادقة عند الحصر الكلي فيكون قولنا زيد المعني يجوز أن يدخله كل فكأنه يقول إن كل شيء يوصف بأنه زيد المعني. وبالجملة إذا أخذت الكبرى مهملة أوهمت الصدق، وإذا جعلت كلية كذبت؛ فهذا المثال وما قبله من جملة ما تكون الحدود فيه مرتبة ترتيبها ولكن في تحصيل كميتها تخليط؛ وربَّما كان التخليط في روابط الحدود. وذلك لأنه ربَّما يعبر عن القياس بأن ابتدئ من المحمولات حينئذ إلى زيادة لفظ ليس داخلا في الحدود بل هو رابطة أو نسبة رابطة ؛ ثمَّ يقع فيه اشتراك كقول من يقول إن النتيجة لا في شيء من المرض، والمرض في كل إنسان قبل، وقد يظن أنه يعرض من هذا أن الصحة غير ممكنة أن تكون في واحد من الناس. ثم لقائل أن يقول: إنه إحدى (١) النتيجة ضرورية ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك وإن كان قياسًا. والذي يظن أن يلزم عنه هو أن الصحة ليست ولا في واحد من الناس فإنه يكون حقا إذا كانت الصغرى وهي قولنا إن المرض في كل إنسان ضرورية. فأما إذا كانت ممكنة خاصة فإنه يجب أن يكون ممكنا، وكذلك إذا كانت الصغرى مطلقة وصح إطلاقها فإنه يجب أن يكون مطلقا . ثم الحالة إذا أخذت مكان ما يقبلها كما إذا أخذت الصحة مكان الصحيح والمرض مكان المريض في القوة وإن لم يكن بالفعل، فهذه أنحاء من الغلط يعرض بسبب الأمور الداخلة على الحدود مثل الحصر والإهمال والربط.
That bird is the very same even if the candle is that candle. And it is not far that a thing differs from itself in its states, and difference is not valid with numerical unity. Then the middle term, when it is not individual, it is appropriate for the major premise to be true under universal quantification, so our saying 'Zaid the meaning' allows that every may enter it, as if he says that everything described as Zaid the meaning. In summary, when the major premise is taken as indefinite, it gives the illusion of truth, and when made universal it is false; this example and the one before are among those where the limits are arranged in their order, but in obtaining their quantity there is mixing; and sometimes the mixing is in the connectives of the limits. That is because sometimes a syllogism is expressed by starting from predicates then adding a word not internal to the limits but is a connective or a relative relation; then ambiguity occurs, as when one says 'the conclusion is not in any disease, and disease is in every human before', and one might think that from this results that health is impossible to be in any person. Then a speaker may say: it is one of the necessary results and it does not necessarily follow, even if it is a syllogism. What one thinks follows is that health is not in any person, and that is true if the minor premise, which is our saying 'disease is in every human', is necessary. But if it is contingent, then it must be contingent, and likewise if the minor premise is absolute and its absoluteness is sound, then it must be absolute. Then the state when something is taken in place of what accepts it, as when health is taken in place of the healthy and disease in place of the patient in potency even if not in actuality; these are kinds of error that occur due to things that enter upon the limits, such as quantification, neglect, and connection.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage critiques common errors in syllogisms arising from improper handling of quantification and connectives. The author begins by noting a strange example about a bird and a candle to illustrate that identity and difference are not merely numerical. He then focuses on the middle term: if the middle term is not taken universally (as a universal term), the major premise must be universally quantified for validity, but then a claim like 'every Zaid' becomes problematic. If the major premise is left indefinite, it seems true; if universal, it becomes false. The author attributes this confusion to mixing in the quantity of the limits or in their connectives. He gives an explicit erroneous syllogism: 'the conclusion is not in any disease, and disease is in every human'—one might think this proves health impossible for anyone. However, the author clarifies that the conclusion depends on the modality of the minor premise: if the minor is necessary, the conclusion is necessary; if contingent, the conclusion is contingent; if absolute, absolute. Thus the error lies in ignoring modal distinctions. He also warns against substituting terms (e.g., 'health' for 'healthy') without considering potency and actuality. In fiqh reasoning, this parallels errors when using a universal rule without checking the modality of the subject, e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, and this drink is an intoxicant'—if the minor premise is not universally necessary, the conclusion may be invalid.

Key Terms
الأوسط
الحصر الكلي
الروابط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the conclusion depend on when the syllogism is valid?
A) The truth of the major premise.
B) The modal status of the minor premise.
C) The number of terms in the syllogism.
D) The quantification of the middle term.

Key Takeaway
The modality of a syllogistic conclusion follows the modality of the minor premise; never assume necessity without checking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the syllogism: 'Every act of usury is forbidden; this transaction is an act of usury; therefore this transaction is forbidden.' Identify the modality of the minor premise and state what modality the conclusion would have. Then explain why it would be a fallacy to claim the conclusion is absolutely necessary without checking the modality of the minor premise.

Ambiguities from Connectives and Substitutions
Author illustrates errors from ambiguous connectives and substitution of terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify errors caused by connectives mistaken for part of the predicate and by improper substitution of abstract terms for concrete ones.

Core Idea
Connectives and substitutions can create fallacious syllogisms; the meaning changes when terms are not used in the same respect.

The author warns that sometimes confusion arises from connectives (rawabit) that are not part of the essential limits but are added, causing ambiguity. Additionally, taking 'health' in place of 'healthy' or 'disease' in place of 'patient' can lead to erroneous conclusions. This lesson will help you recognise such ambiguities in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the connective in the syllogism
Look for words that are not part of the essential terms but serve as relational links, such as prepositions. The author says they can cause ambiguity.
Example: 'in' in 'health is not in any disease'.
2
Check if the connective is mistakenly taken as part of the predicate
If the connective is treated as essential, the meaning may shift. For instance, 'health is not in any disease' might be misinterpreted as 'health is not a property of disease' rather than 'no diseased person is healthy'.
3
Examine substitution errors
The author gives the example of taking 'health' for 'healthy' and 'disease' for 'patient'. This changes the subject from a person (concrete) to an attribute (abstract).
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In fiqh, consider: 'Riba is forbidden; this transaction involves riba; therefore it is forbidden.' If one substitutes 'riba' with 'excess' without specifying the item, the conclusion may be ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Repetition of Terms in Syllogisms and Avoiding Fallacy
Author analyzes repeated terms and warns against equivocation in syllogistic reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a term in a categorical syllogism is repeated and determine whether it functions as the middle term or as part of the predicate, and recognize the fallacy that arises from misattributing the repeated term.

Core Idea
A repeated term in a syllogism may be part of the predicate, not the middle term.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle but critical point in syllogistic reasoning: the ambiguous repetition of terms. Using the example 'Justice is good, and every good is known that it is good', he shows how 'good' appears both as the middle term and as part of the major term. He also critiques a flawed existential argument that results from misplacing the repeated term. This lesson will equip you with the analytical skill to resolve such ambiguities and construct valid syllogisms free from this fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the repeated term in the example
In the syllogism 'Justice is good, and every good is known that it is good, therefore justice is known that it is good', the term 'good' appears twice. Locate each occurrence.
One occurrence is the middle term, the other is part of the major term 'known that it is good'.
2
Determine the logical role of each occurrence
According to the author, the first 'good' functions as the middle term linking justice to being known. The second 'good' is not separate but part of the predicate 'known that it is good'. Use the rule: when a term is said 'by virtue of being that term', it belongs to the predicate.
This rule is crucial for avoiding confusion.
3
Apply the rule to the flawed existential argument
The author presents the argument: 'The existent by virtue of being existent is either self-subsistent or not self-subsistent'. Using the rule, 'by virtue of being existent' should be part of the predicate, not a condition on the subject. Show why this leads to a contradiction if misinterpreted.
The contradiction arises because the division applies to the predicate, not the subject.
4
Recognize the fallacy of misattribution
The fallacy occurs when the repeated term is wrongly taken as a condition on the subject, leading to absurd conclusions. Identify this fallacy in any argument where a term is repeated and its role is ambiguous.
This is a common sophism in syllogistic reasoning.
5
Construct a valid syllogism avoiding the repetition problem
Using the author's advice, ensure that the repeated term (if it appears) is correctly placed in the predicate. For example, to prove 'good is known', take 'good' as something existent and form a syllogism with the condition correctly assigned.
Practice this with the given example to cement the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه قد يعرض لبعض الحدود أن يوجد مكرّرًا فيظن أنه من حيث هو مكرر؛ ولا يكون كذلك كما في قولنا العدل خير، وكل خير فإنه يعلم أنه خير فالعدل يعلم أنه خير فالخير هنا يكون مرة حدا أوسط، ومرة جزءًا من الأكبر فيجب أن تعلم أنه حيث ما قيل من جهة كذا فإنه في المحمول دون الموضوع. فإن قولك «ج» كذا من جهة ما هو «ج» ليس (ج) الثانية جزءًا من الموضوع بل المحمول. ولهذا وقع الغلط في قول من يقول: «إن الموجود من جهة ما موجود إما قائم بنفسه وإما غير قائم بنفسه». ولو كان الموجود من جهة ما هو موجود غير قائم بنفسه لوجب أن يكون كل موجود موصوفا أنه قائم بنفسه. وكذلك إن كان من تلك الجهة قائما بنفسه وجب أن يكون كل موجود قائما بنفسه : وهذا خلف. ولا يعلم أن المقابل للموجبة منهما هو أنه ليس من جهة ما هو موجود قائما بنفسه فيدخل السلب على القائم بنفسه مقرونا به من جهة ما هو موجود. ويكون من جهة ما هو موجود داخلا في المحمول فلا يلزم من ذلك المحال، فجميع أمثال هذه يجب أن يوجد المكرر منها في جملة المحمول. فإن كان في الأوسط في مثل الضرب المشار إليه وقع أيضًا في الأكبر. وإن لم يجر الأمر على هذه الجهة لم يكن للكلام معنى، ولم ينحل إلى الضروب ومن الأمثلة لهذا الباب الإنسان محسوس، وكل محسوس فإنه يعدم من جهة ما هو محسوس؛ وكلما أردت أن ينتج في أمثال هذه الأشياء بشرط فيجب عليك أن تأخذ الأكبر في الأوسط بالشرط ثمَّ تُحلل. وإن صح إنتاجك مرسلًا فخذ ذلك مرسلا ثم حلل مثال الأول إن أردت أن ينتج أن الخير معلوم ما؛ فقل لأنه موجود ما. واعلم بأن حذف شيء يسير يوقع خلافا كثيرًا كما إدخاله يوقع خلافا كثيرًا كما أنك إذا قلت إن اللذة هي خير كان له معنى، وإذا قلت إن اللذة هي
And know that it sometimes occurs to some limits that they are repeated, and one thinks that it is by virtue of being repeated; but it is not so. As in our saying 'Justice is good, and every good is known that it is good, so justice is known that it is good.' Here 'good' is once a middle term and once a part of the major term. So you must know that wherever it is said 'such and such' by virtue of being 'such and such', it is in the predicate, not the subject. For your saying 'Ja is thus by virtue of being Ja' - the second 'Ja' is not part of the subject, but of the predicate. And because of this, error occurred in the saying of one who says: 'The existent by virtue of being existent is either self-subsistent or not self-subsistent.' If the existent by virtue of being existent is not self-subsistent, then it would be necessary that every existent be described as self-subsistent. And likewise if it were self-subsistent by that virtue, it would be necessary that every existent be self-subsistent. This is a contradiction. And one does not know that the opposite of the affirmative of them is that it is not by virtue of being existent self-subsistent, so the negation enters upon 'self-subsistent' conjoined with it by virtue of being existent. And 'by virtue of being existent' enters into the predicate, so that impossible does not follow. So all such cases must have the repeated term in the predicate. For if it were in the middle term in the indicated mode, it would also fall in the major term. And if the matter does not proceed in this manner, the statement has no meaning and does not resolve into the modes. And among the examples for this chapter: 'The human is sentient, and every sentient is annihilated by virtue of being sentient.' And whenever you want to produce a syllogism in such things with a condition, you must take the major term in the middle term with the condition, then analyze. If your production is true absolutely, take it absolutely, then analyze. The first example: if you want that 'good is something known', say because it is something existent. And know that deleting a small thing causes much disagreement, just as adding it causes much disagreement. For example, if you say 'Pleasure is good', it has a meaning; and if you say 'Pleasure is' (cut off).
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of term repetition in syllogisms and the fallacy that can arise when a repeated term is misinterpreted. The author begins with an example: 'Justice is good, and every good is known that it is good, therefore justice is known that it is good.' Here 'good' appears twice: once as the middle term and once as part of the major term ('known to be good'). The key rule is that when a term is said 'by virtue of being that term' (e.g., 'by virtue of being Ja'), it belongs to the predicate, not the subject. The author then critiques a flawed argument about existence: 'The existent by virtue of being existent is either self-subsistent or not self-subsistent.' This leads to a contradiction because if the existent qua existent is not self-subsistent, then everything existent would be self-subsistent, and vice versa. The resolution is that 'by virtue of being existent' is part of the predicate, so the division does not apply to the subject. The author emphasizes that in all such cases, the repeated term must be considered part of the predicate. He also provides instructions for constructing valid syllogisms with conditions and gives an example: to prove 'good is known', say 'because it is something existent.' The passage concludes with a warning that small changes (adding or deleting) cause great disagreement, illustrated by the incomplete example 'Pleasure is good.' This lesson is crucial for understanding how to avoid equivocation in Islamic logical analysis.

Key Terms
مكرر
قائم بنفسه
خلف

A) It is the middle term.
B) It is part of the minor term.
C) It is part of the major term.
D) It is the subject of the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The key lesson is that a repeated term in a syllogism must be carefully analyzed: if it appears in the form 'by virtue of being X', it belongs to the predicate, and misplacing it leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'The Quran is revealed, and everything revealed is created by virtue of being revealed. Therefore, the Quran is created by virtue of being revealed.' Identify the repeated term and determine whether it appears in the middle term or part of the predicate. Is this a valid syllogism? Explain why or why not.
?
Take the flawed existential argument from the lesson and rewrite it correctly by placing the condition 'by virtue of being existent' in the predicate. Then explain why the corrected version avoids the contradiction.

The Definite Article and Syllogistic Validity: Pleasure, Good, and Convertibility
Author analyzes syllogism about pleasure and good, distinguishing indefinite from definite predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an indefinite syllogistic premise is valid by recognizing convertible (coextensive) material.

Core Idea
The definite article can turn a true statement into a false one; indefinite premises are valid only when terms are coextensive.

This passage from a classical logic text examines how a small change—adding or removing the definite article—dramatically alters the truth of a proposition and the validity of a syllogism. The author uses the example of 'pleasure is good' versus 'pleasure is the good' to illustrate this, and then addresses a critic's objection about the use of indefinite premises. You will learn to identify when indefinite premises can yield a valid conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two statements
Compare 'Pleasure is good' (indefinite predicate) with 'Pleasure is the good' (definite predicate). Note how the definite article changes the logical force: the first asserts that pleasure is one good thing; the second asserts that pleasure is identical to goodness itself.
The author says the first is true for one who admits pleasure is good, but the second is false.
2
Identify the syllogistic goal
If the goal is to conclude 'Pleasure is good', the major term must be 'good' without the definite article. If the goal is 'Pleasure is the good', the major term must include the article. This shows the article's impact on the syllogism's terms.
The conclusion's truth depends on correctly choosing the major term.
3
Analyze the critic's objection
The critic says if B is the middle term, then the major premise must be one of three: universal ('Every B is the good'), particular ('Some B is the good'), or indefinite ('B is the good'). Universal is false (implies each B is the whole good), particular makes the major premise particular (not valid in first figure), and indefinite normally gives no conclusion.
The critic assumes standard syllogistic rules where indefinite premises are unproductive.
4
Apply the exception: convertible material
The author replies that indefinite premises do produce a conclusion when the material is convertible—i.e., the terms are coextensive. In this case, 'pleasure' and 'B' are equivalent, and 'B' and 'the good' are equivalent, so conversion yields universal propositions: every pleasure is B, every B is good, etc.
This relies on the property of equivalence (musāwāt).
5
Distinguish 'every strike' from 'all good is B'
The author notes that 'every strike' (referring to subjects of good) is different from 'all good is B' (referring to the nature of good). The latter conveys equivalence, which is necessary for the conversion argument.
This highlights the precision needed in quantifying over natures vs. individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن حذف شيء يسير يوقع خلافا كثيرًا كما إدخاله يوقع خلافا كثيرًا كما أنك إذا قلت إن اللذة هي خير كان له معنى، وإذا قلت إن اللذة هي الخير كان له معنى، وبينهما فرق بعيد؛ إذ الأول منهما صادق عند من سلم أن اللذة خير، والثاني ليس بصادق؛ إذ ذاك يوجب أن يكون للخير مساويًا في الحمل. وكذلك إذا كان المراد أن ينتج أن اللذة هي خير فيجب أن تجعل الأكبر خيرا بغير الألف واللام. وإن كان المراد أن ينتج أن اللذة هي الخير فيجب أن يوجد مع الألف واللام. ثم لقائل أن يقول إن في هذا لخللا فليكن «ب» هو الحد الأوسط فتكون اللذة هي «ب» فلا يخلو إما أن يقال إن كل «ب» هو الخير أو بعض «ب» هو الخير أو الباء هو الخير. أما الأول فإن كذب؛ إذ معناه أن كل واحد مما هو «ب» هو الخير كله. وأما الثاني فالكبرى حينئذ صارت جزئية. وأما الثالث فإنها فيه مهملة فلا تنتج. لكنا نقول في الجواب إن المهملة في المادة المنعكسة تنتج؛ وذلك لأن المحمول لا يكون متحققاً بالبعض من الموضوع دون البعض، والقياس قد يكون من مهملتين في هذه المادة؛ حيث يقال إن اللذة هي الباء، والباء هي الخير. وإنها هي المتساوية فتنعكس فيكون كل لذة «ب» وكل «ب» لذة، وكل «ب» خير، وكل خير «ب». وليكن قولنا كل ضرب لا يفيد إفادة قولنا كل الخير هو الباء؛ فإن الأول يلتفت إلى موضوعات الخير، وهذا يلتفت إلى طبيعة الخير، وإنه يفيد المساواة دون ذلك.
Know that the omission of something small causes much difference, just as its insertion causes much difference. For example, when you say 'Pleasure is good' (al-ladhdha hiya khayr) it has a meaning, and when you say 'Pleasure is the good' (al-ladhdha hiya al-khayr) it has a meaning, and between them is a great difference. For the first of them is true for one who admits that pleasure is good, but the second is not true, because that requires that it be equivalent to good in predication. Likewise, if the intention is to produce the conclusion 'Pleasure is good', you must make the major term 'good' without the definite article. If the intention is to produce the conclusion 'Pleasure is the good', you must have it with the definite article. Then someone may say there is a flaw here: let 'B' be the middle term, so pleasure is 'B'; then it is unavoidable that either every 'B' is the good, or some 'B' is the good, or 'B' is the good. As for the first, it is false, because its meaning is that every single person (thing) that is 'B' is the whole good. As for the second, then the major premise becomes particular. As for the third, it is indefinite and does not produce a conclusion. But we say in answer that the indefinite in convertible material does produce a conclusion. This is because the predicate is not realized for some of the subject without the rest, and a syllogism may consist of two indefinite premises in this material, where one says: 'Pleasure is B, and B is the good.' And since they are equivalent, they convert, so every pleasure is B and every B is pleasure, and every B is good, and every good is B. And our saying 'every strike' does not convey the same as our saying 'all good is B'; for the first refers to the subjects of good, and this refers to the nature of good, and it conveys equivalence without that.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage examines the logical significance of the definite article in predication, specifically comparing 'pleasure is good' (indefinite predicate) with 'pleasure is the good' (definite predicate). The author begins by noting that a small omission or insertion (the definite article) causes a great difference in meaning. The first statement is true for one who admits that pleasure is a good thing, but the second is false unless pleasure is identical to goodness itself. For syllogistic purposes, if we want to conclude 'pleasure is good', the major term must be 'good' without the article; if we want 'pleasure is the good', the major term must include the article. A critic then objects that this syllogism is flawed because if we let B be the middle term (so pleasure is B), then the major premise must be one of three: 'Every B is the good' (universal affirmative, false because it would mean each B is the whole good), 'Some B is the good' (particular, making the major premise particular, which may not yield a conclusion), or 'B is the good' (indefinite, which normally does not produce a conclusion in standard figures). The author responds that an indefinite premise can produce a conclusion in 'convertible material' — where the terms are coextensive. In this case, 'pleasure' and 'B' are equivalent, and 'B' and 'the good' are equivalent. With equivalence, conversion is possible: every pleasure is B, every B is pleasure, every B is good, every good is B. Thus the syllogism works with indefinite premises because the terms are convertible. Finally, the author distinguishes between 'every strike' (which refers to the subjects of good) and 'all good is B' (which refers to the nature of good), noting that the latter conveys equivalence. This analysis highlights the precision required in handling universality and definiteness in logical reasoning.

Composite Syllogisms and the Need for Decomposition
Author introduces a new chapter on obstacles to analyzing syllogisms due to their form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a composite syllogism and explain why its apparent figure may not reflect the figures of its components.

Core Idea
A composite syllogism is a chain of arguments, not a single one.

In the previous discussion, we examined obstacles to analyzing syllogisms that arise from the terms themselves. Now, the author turns to obstacles caused by the form of the syllogism and the form of the premise. The key insight is that some syllogisms are composite (murakkab) – they consist of multiple simpler syllogisms linked together. When we encounter such a composite, we must not be misled by its outward appearance; the figure and mood of the whole do not necessarily indicate the figures of its parts. This lesson introduces the concept of composite syllogisms and sets the stage for learning how to decompose them.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two types of obstacles
The author distinguishes between obstacles due to the terms (hudud) and obstacles due to the form of the syllogism (shakl al-qiyas) or the form of the premise (shakl al-muqaddima). Note that this chapter focuses on the latter.
Obstacles due to terms were covered earlier; now we move to formal obstacles.
2
Identify a composite syllogism
A composite syllogism (qiyas murakkab) is not a single syllogism but a series of syllogisms linked together. The author states that sometimes a given syllogism is composite and needs to be analyzed (taḥlīl) into other syllogisms.
Think of a chain of reasoning where the conclusion of one syllogism becomes a premise for the next.
3
Avoid the common misconception
The author warns: if a syllogism appears to suggest that the second syllogism is of its own figure, do not accept that at face value. Composite syllogisms can involve different figures and moods. Do not assume that the whole shares the figure of its parts.
The form of the composite does not determine the forms of the components.
4
Understand the purpose of decomposition
Analysis (taḥlīl) is the process of breaking a composite syllogism into its constituent simpler syllogisms. This is necessary for evaluating validity and understanding the logical structure.
Only after decomposition can we check each component syllogism for validity.

The author states that a given syllogism may be composite (murakkab), meaning it is composed of multiple simpler syllogisms, and therefore requires decomposition. One must be careful not to assume that the apparent figure of the composite syllogism indicates the figure of its components. Instead, one must recognize that composite syllogisms can involve different figures and moods. The text cuts off here, but the intended continuation likely provides examples and methods for handling such composite syllogisms. This introduction sets the stage for the subsequent detailed discussion.

Syllogistic Proof that Pleasure is Good and Objection
Author discusses a syllogism proving pleasure is good and rebuts an objection about the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and evaluate a categorical syllogism with an indefinite premise in convertible matter, and identify when an indefinite premise yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
An indefinite proposition can yield a conclusion if the terms are co-extensive.

In this passage, the author presents a syllogism aiming to prove that pleasure is good. An objector challenges the syllogism by focusing on the middle term. The author defends by explaining that when the middle term and the predicate are equal in extension (convertible matter), even indefinite premises can produce a valid conclusion. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of the argument and the rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intended conclusion
The author distinguishes between proving that pleasure is 'a good' (indefinite, without definite article) versus 'the good' (definite). This affects how the major term is formulated in the syllogism.
For 'the good', the major premise must include the definite article.
2
Understand the objector's three possibilities
The objector says: if 'B' is the middle term and pleasure is 'B', then the major premise is either 'Every B is the good', 'Some B is the good', or 'B is the good'. Each supposedly fails: the first is false, the second makes the premise particular, the third is indefinite and normally yields no conclusion.
The objector assumes standard syllogistic rules.
3
Grasp the author's reply: convertible matter
The author argues that in 'convertible matter' (al-madda al-mun'akisa), where subject and predicate are co-extensive, an indefinite premise converts to a universal affirmative. Here, 'pleasure' and 'B' are equal (musawiya), and 'B' and 'the good' are equal, so the indefinite premises 'pleasure is B' and 'B is the good' convert to universals.
Co-extensiveness means each term applies to exactly the same individuals.
4
Verify the conversion logic
From the equalities: 'pleasure is B' converts to 'Every pleasure is B'; 'B is the good' converts to 'Every B is the good'. Then the syllogism becomes: Every B is the good, Every pleasure is B, therefore Every pleasure is the good. This is valid in the first figure with universal affirmatives.
The author also notes that 'every good is B' differs from 'every B is good' in focus, but both hold due to equality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخير كان له معنى، وبينهما فرق بعيد؛ إذ الأول منهما صادق عند من سلم أن اللذة خير، والثاني ليس بصادق؛ إذ ذاك يوجب أن يكون للخير مساويًا في الحمل. وكذلك إذا كان المراد أن ينتج أن اللذة هي خير فيجب أن تجعل الأكبر خيرا بغير الألف واللام. وإن كان المراد أن ينتج أن اللذة هي الخير فيجب أن يوجد مع الألف واللام. ثم لقائل أن يقول إن في هذا لخللا فليكن «ب» هو الحد الأوسط فتكون اللذة هي «ب» فلا يخلو إما أن يقال إن كل «ب» هو الخير أو بعض «ب» هو الخير أو الباء هو الخير. أما الأول فإن كذب؛ إذ معناه أن كل واحد مما هو «ب» هو الخير كله. وأما الثاني فالكبرى حينئذ صارت جزئية. وأما الثالث فإنها فيه مهملة فلا تنتج. لكنا نقول في الجواب إن المهملة في المادة المنعكسة تنتج ؛ وذلك لأن المحمول لا يكون متحققاً بالبعض من الموضوع دون البعض، والقياس قد يكون من مهملتين في هذه المادة؛ حيث يقال إن اللذة هي الباء، والباء هي الخير. وإنها هي المتساوية فتنعكس فيكون كل لذة «ب» وكل «ب» لذة، وكل «ب» خير، وكل خير «ب». وليكن قولنا كل ضرب لا يفيد إفادة قولنا كل الخير هو الباء؛ فإن الأول يلتفت إلى موضوعات الخير، وهذا يلتفت إلى طبيعة الخير، وإنه يفيد المساواة دون ذلك.
The good (al-khayr) has a meaning, and between them there is a great difference; for the first of them is true for one who concedes that pleasure is good, and the second is not true; because that necessitates that the good has an equal in predication. Similarly, if the intention is to prove that pleasure is a good (khayr), then the major term must be made 'a good' without the definite article. And if the intention is to prove that pleasure is the good (al-khayr), then it must come with the definite article. Then an objector may say that there is a flaw here: let 'B' be the middle term, making pleasure 'B'. Then it is either said that every B is the good, or some B is the good, or B is the good. As for the first, it is false because it means that every individual that is B is the whole good. As for the second, the major premise then becomes particular. As for the third, it is indefinite (muhmala) and thus does not yield a conclusion. But we say in reply that an indefinite proposition in convertible matter does yield a conclusion; because the predicate is not realized in some of the subject without others, and the syllogism may be from two indefinite premises in this matter, where it is said: pleasure is B, and B is the good. Since they are equal, they convert, so that every pleasure is B and every B is pleasure, and every B is the good and every good is B. Let our statement 'every kind' not convey the same as our statement 'every good is B'; for the first turns to the subjects of good, while this turns to the nature of good, and it conveys equality without that.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange about a syllogism intended to prove that pleasure (al-ladhdha) is good (khayr or al-khayr). The author first distinguishes between two senses: proving that pleasure is 'a good' (indefinite) versus 'the good' (definite), which require different predicates. An objector then proposes to test the argument by letting 'B' be the middle term and pleasure be 'B'. The objector enumerates three possibilities for the major premise: (1) 'Every B is the good' — this is false because it implies each B is the whole good; (2) 'Some B is the good' — this makes the major premise particular, which in the first figure would yield no conclusion; (3) 'B is the good' — an indefinite proposition, which normally yields no conclusion. The author replies that in 'convertible matter' (al-madda al-mun'akisa) where subject and predicate are co-extensive, an indefinite proposition can produce a conclusion. Since pleasure and B are equal (musawiya), the propositions convert to universal affirmatives: every pleasure is B, every B is pleasure, every B is good, every good is B. Thus the syllogism is valid. The author further clarifies that 'every good is B' differs from 'every B is good' in that the former concerns the subjects of good while the latter concerns the nature of good. This passage illustrates how subtle logical considerations (convertibility, definiteness) affect syllogistic validity, relevant to proofs in kalam about moral properties.

Resolving Compound Syllogisms and Refuting Compound Predicates
Author explains how to refute a compound predicate by attacking part of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a compound predicate claim and construct the most efficient refutation by attacking a part of the predicate.

Core Idea
When a claim has a compound predicate, refuting just one part is often simpler than refuting the whole.

In complex arguments, especially in theological and legal debates, claims often involve compound predicates like 'X is a simple drinkable body'. The author shows that instead of attacking the whole predicate, you can target a single part that, if false, makes the whole false. This lesson teaches you how to spot such opportunities and construct a minimal refutational syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound predicate
Look for a predicate that has multiple attributes combined, e.g., 'simple drinkable body'. The claim is 'A is B and C'. Determine which part, if false, would make the whole predicate false.
2
Choose the most efficient part to attack
The author advises refuting the part that is easier to disprove. In the example, refuting 'drinkable' is simpler because it can be shown using a straightforward categorical syllogism with one term.
3
Construct a syllogism against that part
Form a syllogism that proves the part is false. Example: 'Sea water is water; sea water is not drinkable; therefore, sea water is not a drinkable body.' This syllogism replaces the need to refute the whole compound.
The syllogism may be categorical or conditional depending on the claim.
4
Verify that refuting the part suffices
Ensure that if the part is false, the entire compound predicate is false. In the example, 'not drinkable' entails 'not a simple drinkable body' because drinkability is a necessary component of the compound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنشرع الآن في الأحوال المانعة عن التحليل بسبب شكل القياس وشكل المقدمة أعني الذي ليس بسبب الحدود؛ فنقول إنه قد يتفق أن يكون القياس الموجود مركبا فيحتاج أن يحلل إلى الغير من القياسات. فإذا وجد قياس يوهم أن القياس الثاني من شكله فيجب أن لا يلتفت إلى ذلك وتعلم أن القياسات قد تكون متركبة وهي من أشكال وضروب مختلفة بل يجب أن تعبر حال التأليف في نسبته لا غير . فإذا كانت القياسات مؤلفة لتنتج سلبًا وكان المحمول مركبا؛ فإنه يمكن إبطال ذلك التركيب بإبطال الجملة، ويمكن أيضًا بإبطال البعض منها كما إذا كان الدعوى أن الماء جسم بسيط مشروب فيقال ليس جسما بسيطًا مشروبًا أو يقال ليس مشروبًا فإنه إذا لم يكن مشروبًا لم يكن جسما بسيطًا مشروبًا. ثمَّ إبطال أنه مشروب يكفي أن يورد الحد الأكبر في القياس وحده فيكون التحليل أسهل فيقول ماء البحر ماء، وماء البحر ليس بمشروب؛ فهذا يقوم مقام المركب في غرضك. فإن كان القياس استثنائيا فحله إلى شكله لا إلى الأشكال الاقترانية : أما المنتجة للاستثنائي فيمكنك فيه ذلك. وكذلك المنتج للخلف في قياس الخلف إما على الطريق المشهور عند الجمهور وإما الحقيقي المركب من الحملي والشرطي كما مر ؛ وكلاهما اقتراني، ويمكن أن يحلل إلى الأشكال. والفرق بين الشرطية التي في القياس الاستثنائي الذي في الخلف وبين الاستثنائي الذي ليس في الخلف أن الشرطية التي في الخلف لا يلتفت فيها إلى تسليم المخاطب؛ إذ الحق لا يخرج من طرفي النقيض لخلاف ذلك. والخلفية في كل مادة يكون التالي منها نقيض المقدم في الأكبر فلا يصرح بها وفي الغير لا بد من التصريح في أكثر الأمر. وربَّما لم يصرّح وربَّما أشكل الأمر في الانحلال؛ وكان القياس صالحًا إلى أن ينحل إلى أشكال أربعة كالقياس المنتج للسالب الجزئي، أو إلى الأول والثاني كالمنتج الكلي، أو إلى الأول والثالث كالمنتج للموجب الجزئي. وربَّما لا ينحل إلى شكل آخر؛ إما لأنه لا ينتج إلا في شكل واحد كمنتج الموجب الكلي وإما لأنه إن كان ينتج مطلوبه غيره فإنه لا ينحل إلى ذلك الغير؛ لأن حدوده لا يطيع العكس الذي يرد إلييه كرابع الثاني وخامس الثالث. فإنه يجب أن تُراعي الحدود كيف حال
Now we begin on the states that prevent analysis due to the form of the syllogism and the form of the premise, I mean that not due to the limits. We say: it may happen that the existing syllogism is compound and needs to be analyzed into other syllogisms. If a syllogism is found that suggests the second syllogism is of its figure, one should not pay attention to that, and know that syllogisms may be composite and are of different figures and moods, but rather one must consider the state of composition in its relation only. So if syllogisms are composed to produce a negative conclusion and the predicate is compound, then it is possible to refute that composition by refuting the whole, and also by refuting some part of it, as when the claim is that water is a simple drinkable body, then one says: it is not a simple drinkable body, or it is not drinkable, for if it is not drinkable, it is not a simple drinkable body. Then refuting that it is drinkable suffices to introduce the major term alone in the syllogism, making analysis easier, so one says: sea water is water, and sea water is not drinkable; this stands in place of the compound for your purpose. If the syllogism is conditional (istithna'i), its resolution is to its figure, not to the conjunctive figures. As for what produces the conditional, you can do that in it. Similarly, what produces reductio (khalf) in the reductio syllogism — either in the well-known way among the common people or the real way composed of categorical and conditional as previously mentioned — both are conjunctive, and can be analyzed into the figures. The difference between the conditional in the reductio conditional syllogism and the conditional that is not in a reductio is that the conditional in a reductio does not pay attention to the opponent's concession, because truth does not emerge from either side of a contradiction otherwise. The reductio in every matter has its consequent as the contradictory of the antecedent in the major, so it is not stated explicitly; otherwise, explicit statement is necessary in most cases. Sometimes it is not stated, and sometimes the matter of resolution becomes difficult. A syllogism may be fit to be resolved into four figures, like the syllogism that produces a particular negative, or into the first and second like the universal negative, or into the first and third like the particular affirmative. Sometimes it cannot be resolved into another figure, either because it produces only in one figure, like the universal affirmative, or because even if it produces its desired conclusion in another, it cannot be resolved into that other because its limits do not obey the conversion that leads to it, like the fourth of the second and the fifth of the third. So one must consider the limits and how they are.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the obstacles to analyzing syllogisms due to their form (shakl) and the form of the premise. The author distinguishes between obstacles due to limits (hudud) and those due to form. The focus here is on the latter. The main point is that when a syllogism is compound (murakkab), it must be broken down into simpler syllogisms. One should not be misled by appearances that suggest a syllogism belongs to a particular figure. The author then gives an example of a compound predicate: 'water is a simple drinkable body'. To refute this, one can refute the whole or a part; refuting the part (that it is drinkable) is easier and can be done with a simple syllogism: 'sea water is water, and sea water is not drinkable'. This stands in place of the compound. Then the author moves to conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), stating that they should be resolved to their own figure, not to conjunctive figures. For reductio syllogisms (qiyas al-khalf), there are two types: the 'popular' way and the 'real' way which combines categorical and conditional premises. Both are conjunctive in nature and can be analyzed into the figures. A key distinction is made: in a reductio conditional, the opponent's concession is not considered because truth cannot come from accepting both sides of a contradiction. The reductio's consequent is always the contradictory of the antecedent in the major premise, so it is often left implicit; otherwise, it must be stated. Then the author discusses which moods can be resolved into which figures: some resolve into all four (e.g., particular negative), some into first and second (universal negative), some into first and third (particular affirmative). Some cannot be resolved into another figure at all, like the universal affirmative (only in first figure), or because the limits do not accommodate conversion (e.g., the fourth mood of the second figure and fifth of the third). This passage is important for understanding how logicians dissect complex arguments into basic forms, a technique essential for evaluating proofs in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Key Terms
القياس المركب
المحمول المركب
الحد الأكبر

The Syllogism

Conditional and Reductio Syllogisms: Resolution and Distinctive Features
Author explains conditional and reductio syllogisms and their resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a conditional syllogism within a reductio from a regular conditional, and state how each should be resolved.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms resolve to their own figure, and reductio syllogisms ignore the opponent's concession.

Not all syllogisms are categorical; some are conditional (if-then) and some involve reductio ad absurdum. The author clarifies that conditional syllogisms must be analyzed in their own figure, not forced into conjunctive figures. Reductio syllogisms, whether popular or real, are conjunctive in nature and can be analyzed into figures, but they have a special feature: the opponent's concession is irrelevant. This lesson helps you identify these forms and apply the correct resolution method.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the syllogism is conditional (istithna'i)
Look for a premise that is a conditional statement (if...then). The author says such syllogisms must be resolved to their own figure, not to the conjunctive figures of categorical syllogisms.
2
Check if the conditional is part of a reductio (khalf)
A reductio syllogism uses a conditional to derive a contradiction. The author distinguishes two types: popular (common) and real (composed of categorical and conditional premises). Both are conjunctive and can be analyzed into figures.
3
Understand the difference in handling opponent's concession
In a regular conditional syllogism, the opponent's concession (the antecedent or consequent) is used. In a reductio, the opponent's concession is not needed because truth cannot emerge from both sides of a contradiction; the conditional is constructed so that the consequent is the contradictory of the antecedent in the major premise, often left implicit.
4
Determine resolution possibilities
Some syllogisms can be resolved into multiple figures (e.g., particular negative into all four), while others are restricted (e.g., universal affirmative only in first figure). The author gives examples: universal negative to first and second, particular affirmative to first and third. Some cannot be resolved because their limits do not obey conversion.

ولنشرع الآن في الأحوال المانعة عن التحليل بسبب شكل القياس وشكل المقدمة أعني الذي ليس بسبب الحدود؛ فنقول إنه قد يتفق أن يكون القياس الموجود مركبا فيحتاج أن يحلل إلى الغير من القياسات. فإذا وجد قياس يوهم أن القياس الثاني من شكله فيجب أن لا يلتفت إلى ذلك وتعلم أن القياسات قد تكون متركبة وهي من أشكال وضروب مختلفة بل يجب أن تعبر حال التأليف في نسبته لا غير . فإذا كانت القياسات مؤلفة لتنتج سلبًا وكان المحمول مركبا؛ فإنه يمكن إبطال ذلك التركيب بإبطال الجملة، ويمكن أيضًا بإبطال البعض منها كما إذا كان الدعوى أن الماء جسم بسيط مشروب فيقال ليس جسما بسيطًا مشروبًا أو يقال ليس مشروبًا فإنه إذا لم يكن مشروبًا لم يكن جسما بسيطًا مشروبًا. ثمَّ إبطال أنه مشروب يكفي أن يورد الحد الأكبر في القياس وحده فيكون التحليل أسهل فيقول ماء البحر ماء، وماء البحر ليس بمشروب؛ فهذا يقوم مقام المركب في غرضك. فإن كان القياس استثنائيا فحله إلى شكله لا إلى الأشكال الاقترانية : أما المنتجة للاستثنائي فيمكنك فيه ذلك. وكذلك المنتج للخلف في قياس الخلف إما على الطريق المشهور عند الجمهور وإما الحقيقي المركب من الحملي والشرطي كما مر ؛ وكلاهما اقتراني، ويمكن أن يحلل إلى الأشكال. والفرق بين الشرطية التي في القياس الاستثنائي الذي في الخلف وبين الاستثنائي الذي ليس في الخلف أن الشرطية التي في الخلف لا يلتفت فيها إلى تسليم المخاطب؛ إذ الحق لا يخرج من طرفي النقيض لخلاف ذلك. والخلفية في كل مادة يكون التالي منها نقيض المقدم في الأكبر فلا يصرح بها وفي الغير لا بد من التصريح في أكثر الأمر. وربَّما لم يصرّح وربَّما أشكل الأمر في الانحلال؛ وكان القياس صالحًا إلى أن ينحل إلى أشكال أربعة كالقياس المنتج للسالب الجزئي، أو إلى الأول والثاني كالمنتج الكلي، أو إلى الأول والثالث كالمنتج للموجب الجزئي. وربَّما لا ينحل إلى شكل آخر؛ إما لأنه لا ينتج إلا في شكل واحد كمنتج الموجب الكلي وإما لأنه إن كان ينتج مطلوبه غيره فإنه لا ينحل إلى ذلك الغير؛ لأن حدوده لا يطيع العكس الذي يرد إلييه كرابع الثاني وخامس الثالث. فإنه يجب أن تُراعي الحدود كيف حال

Refuting Compound Predicates by Attacking a Part
Author explains refutation of compound predicates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute a compound predicate in a syllogism by attacking a part of it, and construct a simpler syllogism using the major term alone.

Core Idea
You only need to break one part of a compound to break the whole.

Sometimes logical claims have compound predicates like 'simple drinkable body'. The author shows that instead of refuting the entire compound, it suffices to refute a necessary part, and that part can be used alone in a simpler syllogism. This lesson walks you through this method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound predicate in the claim
Look at the predicate of the claim you want to refute. For example, in 'water is a simple drinkable body', the compound predicate is 'simple drinkable body'.
2
Determine which part is essential
Find which part, if false, makes the whole compound false. Here, if water is not drinkable, then it cannot be a simple drinkable body.
3
Construct a simpler syllogism using that part alone
Use the part (e.g., 'drinkable') as the major term. Create a syllogism that shows the part is false for some instance. Example: 'Sea water is water, sea water is not drinkable' implies some water is not drinkable, refuting the claim.
This syllogism is valid (Felapton: EAO in third figure).
4
Verify the refutation is sufficient
Check that denying the part logically denies the whole compound. Since the compound predicate requires all parts, falsifying one part falsifies the whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعبر حال التأليف في نسبته لا غير . فإذا كانت القياسات مؤلفة لتنتج سلبًا وكان المحمول مركبا؛ فإنه يمكن إبطال ذلك التركيب بإبطال الجملة، ويمكن أيضًا بإبطال البعض منها كما إذا كان الدعوى أن الماء جسم بسيط مشروب فيقال ليس جسما بسيطًا مشروبًا أو يقال ليس مشروبًا فإنه إذا لم يكن مشروبًا لم يكن جسما بسيطًا مشروبًا. ثمَّ إبطال أنه مشروب يكفي أن يورد الحد الأكبر في القياس وحده فيكون التحليل أسهل فيقول ماء البحر ماء، وماء البحر ليس بمشروب؛ فهذا يقوم مقام المركب في غرضك.
It concerns the state of composition in its relation only. So when syllogisms are composed to produce a negation and the predicate is compound, then it is possible to refute that compound by refuting the whole, and it is also possible by refuting a part of it, as when the claim is that water is a simple drinkable body, so it is said: it is not a simple drinkable body, or it is said: it is not drinkable, for if it is not drinkable then it is not a simple drinkable body. Then, refuting that it is drinkable is sufficient by bringing the major term alone in the syllogism, so the analysis becomes easier, so one says: sea water is water, and sea water is not drinkable; so this stands in the place of the compound for your purpose.

Commentary
This passage discusses a method for refuting compound predicates in syllogisms. The author explains that when a syllogism aims to produce a negative conclusion and the predicate is compound, one can attack either the whole compound or a part of it. The example given is the claim 'water is a simple drinkable body.' One can refute it by denying it entirely or by denying just the part 'drinkable' since if it is not drinkable, it cannot be a simple drinkable body. Furthermore, refuting the part is easier because one can bring the major term alone in a syllogism, e.g., 'sea water is water, and sea water is not drinkable,' which effectively replaces the compound predicate.

Resolving Hypothetical and Reductio Syllogisms into Figures
Author explains reduction of hypothetical and reductio syllogisms, and their resolution into figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the figure resolution pattern for a given syllogism type (e.g., particular negative resolves into four figures) and recognize cases where reduction is blocked.

Core Idea
Not all syllogisms reduce neatly—some resist reduction due to term structure.

This passage delves into the technical process of resolving hypothetical and reductio ad absurdum syllogisms into classical syllogistic figures. Unlike purely categorical syllogisms, these types have their own rules for reduction. The author clarifies that hypothetical syllogisms resolve to their own figure, while reductio syllogisms are conjunctive and can be resolved into categorical figures, but with important exceptions. Understanding this resolution is key to mastering advanced syllogistic theory as applied in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism type
Determine if the syllogism is hypothetical (contains a conditional premise as major) or a reductio ad absurdum (proof by contradiction). The passage treats both.
Hypothetical syllogisms are resolved to their own figure, not to categorical figures.
2
Recognize the two types of reductio
The author mentions two methods: the well-known popular method and the real method composed of categorical and conditional premises. Note that both are conjunctive and can be resolved into categorical figures.
3
Learn the resolution patterns
The passage lists patterns: a syllogism producing a particular negative can resolve into all four figures; universal conclusions resolve into first and second; particular affirmatives into first and third.
These patterns depend on the type of conclusion.
4
Identify cases that resist reduction
Some syllogisms resist reduction because they produce a conclusion in only one figure (e.g., universal affirmative) or because their terms do not permit the necessary conversion (e.g., the fourth mood of the second figure and the fifth of the third).
The terms 'do not obey the converse' means conversion is not valid for those moods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان القياس استثنائيا فحله إلى شكله لا إلى الأشكال الاقترانية : أما المنتجة للاستثنائي فيمكنك فيه ذلك. وكذلك المنتج للخلف في قياس الخلف إما على الطريق المشهور عند الجمهور وإما الحقيقي المركب من الحملي والشرطي كما مر ؛ وكلاهما اقتراني، ويمكن أن يحلل إلى الأشكال. والفرق بين الشرطية التي في القياس الاستثنائي الذي في الخلف وبين الاستثنائي الذي ليس في الخلف أن الشرطية التي في الخلف لا يلتفت فيها إلى تسليم المخاطب؛ إذ الحق لا يخرج من طرفي النقيض لخلاف ذلك. والخلفية في كل مادة يكون التالي منها نقيض المقدم في الأكبر فلا يصرح بها وفي الغير لا بد من التصريح في أكثر الأمر. وربَّما لم يصرّح وربَّما أشكل الأمر في الانحلال؛ وكان القياس صالحًا إلى أن ينحل إلى أشكال أربعة كالقياس المنتج للسالب الجزئي، أو إلى الأول والثاني كالمنتج الكلي، أو إلى الأول والثالث كالمنتج للموجب الجزئي. وربَّما لا ينحل إلى شكل آخر؛ إما لأنه لا ينتج إلا في شكل واحد كمنتج الموجب الكلي وإما لأنه إن كان ينتج مطلوبه غيره فإنه لا ينحل إلى ذلك الغير؛ لأن حدوده لا يطيع العكس الذي يرد إليه كرابع الثاني وخامس الثالث.
If the syllogism is hypothetical, its resolution is to its own figure, not to the conjunctive figures. As for what produces the hypothetical, you can do that in it. Likewise, what produces the reductio in a reductio syllogism—either according to the well-known way among the masses or the real composed of categorical and conditional as previously—both are conjunctive and can be resolved into the figures. The difference between the conditional in the hypothetical syllogism that is in reductio and the hypothetical that is not in reductio is that the conditional in reductio does not take into account the opponent's admission, because truth does not exit from the two sides of contradiction, contrary to that. The reductio, in every matter, its consequent is the contradictory of the antecedent in the major, so it is not made explicit, and in other matters it must be made explicit in most cases. Sometimes it is not made explicit, and sometimes the matter becomes difficult in the resolution; and the syllogism is suitable to be resolved into four figures, like the syllogism producing a particular negative, or into the first and second like the universal product, or into the first and third like the product of the particular affirmative. And sometimes it does not resolve into another figure, either because it only produces in one figure like the product of the universal affirmative, or because if it produces its sought conclusion elsewhere, it does not resolve into that other, because its terms do not obey the converse by which it is reduced, like the fourth of the second and the fifth of the third.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage discusses the resolution of hypothetical and reductio syllogisms. The author states that a hypothetical syllogism is resolved into its own figure (i.e., the hypothetical figure) rather than the conjunctive (categorical) figures. He then turns to reductio ad absurdum (خلف), noting two types: the well-known popular method and the real method composed of categorical and conditional premises. Both are conjunctive and can be resolved into categorical figures. The key difference between the conditional in a reductio and a regular hypothetical is that in reductio, the conditional does not rely on the opponent's admission because truth is exclusively one of the two contradictories. The consequent in a reductio conditional is the contradictory of the antecedent in the major premise, so the major is often left implicit. The passage then lists various ways syllogisms can be resolved into the four figures: particular negative conclusions can be resolved into all four, universal conclusions into the first and second, particular affirmatives into first and third. Some syllogisms resist reduction because they only produce a conclusion in one figure (e.g., universal affirmative) or because their terms do not permit the necessary conversion (e.g., the fourth mood of the second figure and the fifth of the third).

The Syllogism

Privative Terms and the True Middle Term
Author demonstrates and critiques Ibn Sina on privatized terms as middle terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critique Ibn Sina's claim that privative terms can serve as middle terms in a syllogism, using the author's distinction between negation by nature and negation by generality.

Core Idea
Privative terms are not true middles; the middle term requires negation from both extremes by nature, not by generality.

The author sets up a complex logical demonstration with variables to show that treating privative terms (like 'not-white') as middle terms leads to contradictions. He then quotes Ibn Sina's own admission of confusion and provides a corrective: the true middle term is that from which both extremes are negated in terms of their natures, not merely in terms of generality. This lesson walks you through the demonstration and the critique, sharpening your ability to evaluate definitions of the middle term in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the variables and their definitions
The author defines A as 'composed', J as 'white', B as 'not-composed' (privatized), D as 'not-white' (privatized). Note that B and D are privative terms, not simple negatives. He distinguishes the privatized from the negative.
Privative terms are like 'not-white' meaning lacking whiteness, not simply the negation of the proposition.
2
Follow the demonstration of generality relations
The author shows that if A (composed) is more general than J (white), then D (not-white) is more general than B (not-composed). He uses truth conditions: when A is false, J is false, so D is true; when B is true, D is true, but A can be true of things that are not B or J. This leads to the conclusion that D is more general than B.
This mirrors standard logical relations between universals and particulars.
3
Identify the reversal and the contradiction (khulf)
The author then reverses the demonstration, treating B as the contradictory of A and D as contradictory of J. He shows that under this assumption, B would be both more general and not more general than D, which is a contradiction. This reveals the fallacy of treating privative terms as whether they were simple contradictories.
The contradiction (khulf) is a key tool for invalidating an assumption.
4
Understand Ibn Sina's admission and the author's critique
The author quotes Ibn Sina from al-Shifa, who admits not fully understanding this reasoning. The author then criticizes Ibn Sina, arguing that the privative terms are not true middle terms. A true middle term (wāsita) is that from which both extremes are negated in terms of their natures (bi ḥaythu al-ṭabīʿatayn), not in terms of generality and particularity.
The author points out that the privatized (maʿdūla) is not a negative proposition; it behaves differently.
5
Apply the correct definition of the middle term
The author concludes that the true middle term must be opposed to each extreme absolutely (mukhālifan li-kull wāḥid min al-ṭarafayn muṭlaqan). Privative terms like 'not-white' are not such absolutes because they do not negate the nature itself but only a property.
This ensures the middle term's role in syllogistic validity.

So let A be 'composed', B 'not possible' (it is not composed), J 'white', D 'not white', B is 'not white', so that B is the thing which is not white, and it is also not 'not white' because the privatized is not the negative, and D is the thing which is not composed, and it is also not 'not composed' because the privatized is not the negative. And if A is composed, then it is predicated of J, the white, as the more general, so that every white is composed, but not every composed is white. And let B be the contradictory of A, which is not composed, and D the contradictory of J, which is not white; then because A is more general than J, D is more general than B. You know, and what increases for you in the explanation of the relation is that you know that since when A is false of something, J is false of it, then D is true of it; and B was true, and whenever B is true, D is true, but A may be true of what is not B J because it is more general. So it is true of some things that are D; then B is false. And whenever B is true, D is true; so D is more general than B. And likewise if D is more general than B, then J is more specific than A. And according to the reverse of that demonstration, let B, the not-composed, be in the power of the contradictory of the composed, which is A; for if it is in the power of the contradictory, then it is subject to this rule in conversion, and let B also be 'to J'. And since D is neither A nor B, and likewise B is neither J nor D, and the extremes are not negated of them. And what is like that is in the status of the middle term, and the middle term is not more entitled to be a contradictory of one extreme than to be a contradictory of the other, rather it is a contradictory of each extreme. So then D, inasmuch as it is a contradictory of A, and B is a contradictory of J, so B is more general than D. And because D is a contradictory of B and B is a contradictory of J, and B was more general than D, so B is more general than D, which is the same: this is a contradiction. All these are among what the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] established in al-Shifa, and he said after: 'As for me, I say: I do not understand this speech with full understanding, and someone else will understand it with a more profound understanding than mine.' Nevertheless, this is not like the middle term in reality; for the middle term is that from which both extremes are negated in terms of the two natures, not in terms of generality and particularity, and the privatized is not the negative in the sense that one says 'Man is not animal' meaning that animal is more general than man; for the affirmative is not predicated of the privatized and the negative opposite to it is predicated of it. In summary, speech in this place requires a detailed investigation of the middle term: what it is and how it is. For if it is an expression of what is opposed to each of the two extremes absolutely, then the privatized...
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a complex logical demonstration and a critique of Ibn Sina's position on privatized propositions. The author begins by setting up variables: A (composed), B (not-composed), J (white), D (not-white). He defines B and D as privative terms, emphasizing that they are not negatives but privatized (ma'dula). He then establishes that A is more general than J (all white is composed, but not vice versa). From this he deduces that D (not-white) must be more general than B (not-composed). He provides reasoning using truth and falsity: when A is false, J is false, and D is true; B is true, and whenever B is true, D is true, but A may be true of things that are not B or J, leading to contradictions. Then he reverses the demonstration, treating B as the contradictory of A and D as contradictory of J, and shows that this leads to a contradiction (khulf) because B would be both more general and not more general. The author then quotes Ibn Sina from al-Shifa, who admits he does not fully understand this reasoning. The author critiques Ibn Sina, arguing that this is not a true middle term (wāsita). A true middle term is that from which both extremes are negated in terms of their natures, not in terms of generality. The privatized is not a negative in the sense that 'man is not animal' would mean animal is more general; rather, the affirmative is not predicated of a privatized term, but (the affirmative) is predicated of its negative counterpart. The author concludes that the matter requires a thorough investigation of what the middle term really is.

Key Takeaway
The middle term in a syllogism must be negated from both extremes in terms of their essential natures, not merely in terms of generality; privative terms do not meet this condition.

The True Middle Term: A Critical Refinement of Ibn Sina's Account
Author critiques Ibn Sina's explanation of the middle term and clarifies its definition with respect to negated terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of the true middle term (al-wāsiṭa) and explain why Ibn Sina's proof is inadequate according to the author.

Core Idea
The middle term is what is denied from both extremes by nature, not by generality.

Ibn Sina proposed a proof to characterize the middle term using a structure of contradictions, but he himself admitted he did not fully understand it. Our author steps in to clarify: the real middle term must be such that both extremes are denied from it absolutely, considering their intrinsic natures. This distinction matters greatly for evaluating syllogisms in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Locate where the author attributes a proof to Ibn Sina from al-Shifa'. The passage begins with a formal demonstration using variables A, B, C, D and ends with a contradiction (khalf).
Ibn Sina's own words: 'لست أفهم هذا الكلام حق الفهم'.
2
Analyze the proof's structure
Follow the relationships: B is true of D, A is more general than some not-B, etc. The proof aims to show that certain relations lead to a contradiction. Identify the contradiction: 'فيكون «ب» أعم من «د» وهو هو: فهذا خلف'.
The variables likely represent terms like 'animal', 'human', etc., but the author keeps them abstract.
3
Extract the author's refined definition
Find the sentence where he defines the true middle term: 'فإن الواسطة هي التي يسلب عنها الطرفان جميعًا من حيث الطبيعتان لا من حيث العموم والخصوص'.
The denial must be by nature, not by relative generality.
4
Distinguish negated terms (ma'dula)
Understand why the ma'dula (e.g., 'non-animal') is not automatically the middle term. The author explains that an affirmative predicate is not carried on the negated term, and the denial of both extremes must be absolute.
Theologians debated whether the negated term can serve as a middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The major premise 'B' is true of 'D', but (A) may be true of what is not 'B' because it is more general. So it is true of some of what is 'D'; then 'B' is false. And whenever 'B' is true, 'D' is true; so 'D' is more general than 'B'. And likewise if 'D' is more general than (B) then (C) is more specific than 'A'. And on the modification of that proof, let (B) be the non-component in the strength of the contradictory of the component which is 'A'; so if it is in the strength of the contradictory, then it is subject to this rule of conversion, and let (B) be likewise 'L J'. And since 'D' is neither 'A' nor 'B', and likewise 'B' is neither 'C' nor 'D', and neither of the two extremes is denied of them. And whatever is like that is in the status of the middle term. The middle term is not more worthy of being the contradictory of one extreme than of being the contradictory of the other, rather it is the contradictory of every extreme. So then (D) from the perspective of being the contradictory of (A) is 'A', and (B) is the contradictory of 'C'; thus (B) is more general than (D). And because 'D' is the contradictory of 'B' and 'B' is the contradictory of 'C', and (B) is more general than (D), so (B) is more general than (D) — that is a contradiction. These are all among what the shaykh established in al-Shifa', and he said afterward: 'As for me, I say: I do not understand this speech fully, and someone other than me will understand it with a more thorough understanding than mine. Nevertheless, this is not like the real middle term; for the middle term is that from which both extremes are denied entirely from the perspective of the two natures, not from the perspective of generality and specificity. And the negated term is not the negative in the way that it is said 'Human is not animal' meaning animal is more general than human. So the affirmative is not predicated of the negated term, while the negative opposite of it is predicated of it. In sum, speech in this context requires a refinement of the investigation: what is the middle term and how is it? For if it is defined as that which is opposed to each of the two extremes absolutely, then the negated term is the middle term; otherwise we do not know whether it is the middle term or not. And among theologians there is speech about that: some affirm it and some deny it, as is known.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of Ibn Sina's account of the middle term (al-wāsiṭa) in syllogistic theory. The author begins by laying out a proof using placeholder variables (A, B, C, D) to show relationships of generality and contradiction. The proof aims to show that if certain conditions hold, a contradiction (khalf) arises. This proof is attributed to the shaykh (Ibn Sina) in al-Shifa'. The author then quotes Ibn Sina's own admission that he does not fully understand the proof. Following this, the author offers his own clarification: the true middle term is that from which both extremes are denied from the perspective of their natures, not merely in terms of generality and specificity. He distinguishes the negated term (al-ma'dūla), which is a term modified with 'ghayr' (non-), from the simple negative proposition. The affirmative predicate is not carried on the negated term, whereas the negative opposite is. Finally, the author notes that among theologians (ahl al-kalām) there is disagreement whether the negated term qualifies as a middle term. This passage refines the definition of the middle term by emphasizing that the negation involved is a denial of both extremes absolutely, not a relative generality.

المعدولة
النقيض

D) It is more general than both extremes.

Key Takeaway
The middle term is defined by the absolute denial of both extremes by nature, not by relative generality or by being a negated term alone.

The Syllogism

Actual vs. Potential Conclusions in First-Figure Universal Syllogisms
Author shows that a first-figure syllogism with universal premises proves the predicate potentially for all things that fall under the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a first-figure categorical syllogism with universal premises yields conclusions potentially for all individuals under the middle term, and distinguish actual (bi-l-fi'l) from potential (bi-l-quwwa) logical derivation.

Core Idea
First-figure universal premises unlock conclusions for everything under the middle term, not just the minor subject.

This passage expands on the power of universal premises in the first figure. You already know that a valid first-figure syllogism gives a certain conclusion for the given subject. Now the author shows that if the major premise is universal, the conclusion is potentially true for any subject that falls under the middle term. This illustrates the difference between actual (bi-l-fi'l) and potential (bi-l-quwwa) logical derivation—a key concept for extending arguments in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the syllogism structure
Find the major term (predicate of conclusion), minor term (subject of conclusion), and middle term. In the example: major = حساس (sensitive), minor = إنسان (human), middle = حيوان (animal). The premises: كل إنسان حيوان (Every human is an animal) and كل حيوان حساس (Every animal is sensitive). Conclusion: كل إنسان حساس (Every human is sensitive).
This is first figure, mood Barbara: all A are B, all B are C, so all A are C.
2
2. Distinguish actual and potential
The syllogism actually (بالفعل) proves the conclusion for the specific minor term (human). But because the major premise is universal affirmative, any subject that falls under the middle term (animal) would also have the predicate (sensitive). This potential (بالقوة) derivation does not require a new syllogism—it is implicit in the truth of the major premise.
The author says 'قام بالفعل' and 'قام بالقوة'.
3
3. Extend to other subjects
The author explicitly says it potentially proves sensitivity for every horse (فرس) and every inanimate object (جماد) if they are animals. This shows the scope of the universal major: anything that shares the middle term inherits the predicate, provided the major premise is true.
This principle underlies qiyas (analogical reasoning) in fiqh: if the 'illah (middle) applies, the hukm (major) applies.
4
4. Apply to negative major premise
Although the text cuts off, the same principle applies if the major is universal negative. For example, if every animal is not sensitive, then potentially everything under animal is not sensitive. This uniformity makes the first figure powerful for both affirmation and negation.
Complete the principle: universal major premises (affirmative or negative) extend the conclusion to all subjects under the middle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هنا وجوها أخرى(۱) من اللوازم المستنبطة من بعض الأقيسة؛ وذلك لأن القياس في الشكل الأول إذا كانت مفردة كلية فإنه إذا قام بالفعل على ثبوت الأكبر لكل الأصغر قام بالقوة على ثبوته لكل ما يشارك الأصغر في الاندراج تحت الأوسط مثلا إذا قام قولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس بالفعل على أن كل إنسان حساس قام أيضًا بالقوة على أن كل فرس حساس، وعلى أن كل جماد حساس. وبالجملة على ثبوت الحساسية لجميع ما يكون مندرجا تحت الحيوان إذا كان المراد هو الحساسية بحس اللمس. وذلك لأن صدق الكبرى يقتضي ذلك. وكذلك إذا كانت الكبرى سالبة فإنه
Know that there are other aspects of necessary consequences derived from some syllogisms. That is because the syllogism in the first figure, when its premises are universal singulars, then if it actually proves the attribution of the major term to every minor term, it also potentially proves it for everything that shares with the minor term in falling under the middle term. For example, if we actually have 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is sensitive' to prove that every human is sensitive, then it also potentially proves that every horse is sensitive, and every inanimate object is sensitive. In sum, it proves sensitivity for everything that falls under animal, if the intended meaning is sensitivity by the sense of touch. That is because the truth of the major premise requires that. Likewise if the major premise is negative...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage adds a further point about the implications of universal premises in the first figure. The author states that when a first-figure syllogism has universal premises (singular universal? 'مفردة كلية' might mean universal singular terms), then the conclusion not only holds for the specific subject of the minor term but also potentially holds for any other subject that shares the same inclusion under the middle term. He gives the example: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every animal is sensitive' (by the sense of touch) actually prove that every human is sensitive, but potentially (bi-l-quwwa) it also proves that every horse is sensitive and every inanimate object is sensitive — because the major premise 'Every animal is sensitive' is true, so anything that is an animal is sensitive. This is essentially a statement about the logical expansion of the conclusion when the major premise is universal affirmative. The author notes that the same applies if the major premise is negative (but the text cuts off). This passage illustrates the notion of potential (quwwa) vs. actual (fi'l) logical derivation.

بالقوة
الشكل الأول

The Syllogism
