Refutation Methods in the Second Figure of Categorical Syllogisms
Author explains how to refute premises in second figure syllogisms using opposite or contradictory of conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute a premise in a second figure categorical syllogism by using the opposite or contradictory of the conclusion.

Core Idea
In the second figure, refuting a premise requires adding the contradictory of the conclusion to one premise to produce a contradiction of the other premise.

The second figure of categorical syllogisms has the middle term as the predicate in both premises. When you want to test a premise's truth, one method is to assume the opposite or contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with one premise. This lesson explains how to do that, showing which combinations work and which fail, using the author's systematic approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the two premises and the conclusion in the second figure syllogism. Note that the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
Example: every J is B, no A is B, therefore no J is A.
2
Determine the opposite and contradictory of the conclusion
The opposite (ضد) is the universal of the same quality (e.g., if conclusion is no J is A, opposite is every J is A). The contradictory (نقيض) is the particular of the opposite quality (e.g., some J is A).
3
Add the contradictory to the minor premise
Take the contradictory of the conclusion and add it to the minor premise. Check if this yields a valid syllogism (usually from the third figure) whose conclusion contradicts the major premise.
This method works for all cases presented by the author.
4
Add the contradictory to the major premise
Take the contradictory of the conclusion and add it to the major premise. This yields a first figure syllogism that contradicts the minor premise.
5
Note limitations with the opposite
The opposite (ضد) does not always work because it may produce a syllogism with two particular premises, which is not valid.
The author recommends using the contradictory for a more reliable refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] وأما في الشكل الثاني فنقول : كل (ج) ب ، ولا شيء من «أب» فلا شيء من (ج) أ»، فإذا أخذ مقابل النتيجة بالضد أو النقيض فيقال كل ج أ) أو بعض (ج) أ) وأضيف إلى الصغرى ينتج من الشكل الثالث بعض «أب»؛ وإنه يناقض الكبرى، ولا يمكن إبطال الكبرى بالضد لما أن القياس من الشكل الثالث: وذلك لا ينتج الكلي. وإذا أضيف إلى الكبرى ينتج من الشكل الأول ما يلزم منه نقيض الصغرى وهو الضد، أو عين النقيض إذا كان ما يضاف إليه نقيضها. أو نقول لا شيء من (ج) ب)، وكل ب أ) فلا شيء من (ج أ»؛ ثم إذا أخذ مقابل النتيجة بالضد أو النقيض وأضيف إلى الصغرى ينتج من الشكل الثالث ما يناقض الكبرى؛ ولا ينتج ما يضادها لما مر . وإذا أضيف إلى الكبرى ينتج من الشكل الأول ما يضاد الصغرى إن كان المضاف إلى الكبرى هو الضد، أو ما يناقضها إن كان هو النقيض. وأما إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة جزئية والكبرى سالبة كلية ينتج ليس بعض «ج أ»، فإذا أخذ المقابل بالنقيض وأضيف إلى الصغرى ينتج من الشكل الثالث ما يناقض الكبرى. وإذا أخذ بالضد فلا يلزم منه ما يُبطل الكبرى؛ إذ لا قياس عن جزئيتين. وكذلك إذا أضيف إلى نقيض الكبرى فإنه ينتج من الشكل الأول ما يناقض الصغرى. وإن أضفناها إلى الضد ينتج من الشكل الأول سالبة جزئية وإنها لا يُبطل الصغرى فكذلك نقول بعض (ج) ليس «ب»، وكل (أ ب) فبعض «ج» ليس «أ»، فإذا أضيف المقابل بالنقيض إلى الصغرى ينتج من الثالث ما يناقض الكبرى. وإذا أضيف إلى الكبرى ينتج من الأول ما يناقض الصغرى؛ والمقابل بالضد لا ينتج مع الصغرى، ومع الكبرى ينتج موجبة جزئية من الأول، وإنها لا تبطل الصغرى.
[B] As for the second figure, we say: every J is B, and no A is B, therefore no J is A. Then if one takes the opposite or contradictory of the conclusion, saying every J is A or some J is A, and adds it to the minor, it produces from the third figure some A is B; and that contradicts the major. And it is not possible to falsify the major by the opposite because the syllogism is from the third figure, and that does not produce a universal. And if added to the major, it produces from the first figure what necessitates the contradictory of the minor, which is the opposite, or the very contradictory if what is added is its contradictory. Or we say: no J is B, and every A is B, therefore no J is A. Then if one takes the opposite or contradictory of the conclusion and adds it to the minor, it produces from the third figure what contradicts the major; and it does not produce what contradicts it [the major] by the opposite for the reason mentioned. And if added to the major, it produces from the first figure what contradicts the minor if what is added to the major is the opposite, or what contradicts it if it is the contradictory. And if the minor is particular affirmative and the major universal negative, it produces: not some J is A. Then if one takes the opposite by the contradictory and adds it to the minor, it produces from the third figure what contradicts the major. And if one takes by the opposite, it does not necessitate that which falsifies the major, because there is no syllogism from two particulars. Likewise, if we add it to the contradictory of the major, it produces from the first figure what contradicts the minor. And if we add it to the opposite, it produces from the first figure a particular negative, and it does not falsify the minor. Similarly, we say: some J is not B, and every A is B, therefore some J is not A. Then if the opposite by the contradictory is added to the minor, it produces from the third figure what contradicts the major. And if added to the major, it produces from the first figure what contradicts the minor. And the opposite by the opposite does not produce with the minor, and with the major it produces a particular affirmative from the first figure, and it does not falsify the minor.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage explains refutation techniques for the second figure of categorical syllogisms. The author covers three main cases: (1) universal affirmative minor and universal negative major, (2) universal negative minor and universal affirmative major, and (3) particular affirmative minor and universal negative major, with a final subcase of particular negative minor and universal affirmative major. For each, he shows that taking the opposite (ضد) or contradictory (نقيض) of the conclusion and combining it with one of the premises can produce a contradiction of the other premise, thereby refuting it. For example, in the first case, taking the contradictory conclusion (some J is A) and adding it to the minor yields a third figure syllogism that contradicts the major. However, using the opposite (every J is A) with the major produces a first figure argument that contradicts the minor. The author notes limitations: the opposite of the conclusion in the second figure is universal, so when combined with the minor (which is also universal) it often works, but when combined with the major it may produce a particular or fail. He emphasizes that in some subcases, using the opposite leads to unproductive syllogisms (e.g., two particulars), so the contradictory is preferred. The analysis systematically demonstrates the validity testing method across different premise combinations.

Refuting a premise in the second figure requires careful selection of the contradictory or opposite of the conclusion, and the contradictory is generally more reliable than the opposite.

Why the Opposite Fails in the Third Figure
Why the opposite (ضد) fails in third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the opposite (ضد) of the conclusion in a third-figure syllogism is particular and how that particularity prevents refutation of the premises.

Core Idea
The opposite of a third-figure conclusion is particular, making refutational syllogisms invalid.

In the third figure, conclusions are always particular. When we attempt to refute the premises by taking the opposite of the conclusion, we find that opposite is also particular. This particularity causes the major premise to be particular in the resulting first- and second-figure syllogisms, violating their validity conditions. Hence, the opposite cannot falsify either premise.

Guided Path
1
Recall third-figure conclusions
Third-figure syllogisms always yield a particular conclusion (affirmative or negative). For example, 'Some scholars are teachers' is particular affirmative.
This is a fixed property of the third figure.
2
Identify the opposite (ضد) of that conclusion
According to the passage, the opposite of a third-figure conclusion is itself particular. For instance, if the conclusion is 'Some scholars are teachers', its opposite is 'Some scholars are not teachers' (particular negative).
This is a key claim in the text; it may differ from other logical conventions.
3
Combine the opposite with each premise
The opposite is taken as a new major premise. First, combine it with the original minor premise to form a first-figure syllogism. Then combine it with the original major premise to form a second-figure syllogism.
The passage states both arrangements are attempted.
4
Check validity conditions for the resulting figures
In the first figure, the major premise must be universal. In the second figure, the major premise must also be universal. However, the opposite is particular, so in both cases the major premise is particular, violating the condition. Thus, no valid syllogism is produced.
This is why the opposite fails to refute.
5
Conclude that the opposite cannot falsify premises
Because the resulting syllogisms are invalid, the opposite does not force a contradiction with the premises. Therefore, the original premises remain unchallenged.
The refutation attempt is blocked by the particularity of the opposite.

Source Text

And its arrangement with the minor in the form of the first figure, and with the major in the form of the second figure, and in any case the major becomes particular; and that prevents production in these two figures. And if one takes the opposite of the conclusion...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage provides a general principle: in the third figure, using the opposite (ضد) of the conclusion cannot refute either premise. The reason is that the conclusion of the third figure is always particular (affirmative or negative). Its opposite is the corresponding universal (e.g., 'Every A is B' is the opposite of 'Some A is B'). When this universal opposite is combined with the minor premise, it forms a first figure syllogism, but the minor is already particular, leading to an invalid combination. Similarly, combining with the major forms a second figure syllogism, but the major becomes particular, again invalid. Therefore, the opposite fails to produce a valid refutation. The author begins to discuss the case of taking the contradictory (نقيض), but the text is cut off. Nevertheless, the key point is clear: in the third figure, the particularity of the conclusion makes the opposite useless for refutation, unlike in other figures where the opposite can be universal and effective.

The Syllogism

Third Figure Refutation: Why Contrary Fails and Contradictory Succeeds
Author explains rule for refuting premises in third figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the contrary of a conclusion in a third-figure categorical syllogism cannot refute any premise, while the contradictory can, and apply this rule to evaluate refutation strategies.

Core Idea
In the third figure, only the universal contradictory of the conclusion refutes a premise; the particular contrary is powerless.

In the third figure, the conclusion is always particular. When attempting to refute a premise by assuming the opposite of the conclusion, you must choose carefully. The contrary (ضد) of a particular proposition is itself particular, and when combined with the premises, it violates the rules of the first and second figures. The contradictory (نقيض), however, is always universal and can produce valid syllogisms. This passage explains why and how. Mastering this rule sharpens your dialectical skills in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third figure and its conclusion type
Recall that the third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises, and its conclusion is always particular (juz'iyya). For example: 'Every A is B; every A is C; therefore some B is C.' The conclusion 'some B is C' is particular.
The conclusion is never universal in the third figure.
2
Distinguish contrary (ḍidd) from contradictory (naqīḍ)
The contrary of a universal affirmative is universal negative; the contradictory of a universal affirmative is particular negative. But here the conclusion is particular, so its contrary is a particular proposition of opposite quality (e.g., 'some B is C' → contrary is 'some B is not C', which is also particular). Its contradictory is universal (e.g., 'no B is C' or 'every B is C' depending on quality). The key: the contrary stays particular, the contradictory becomes universal.
In the third figure, the conclusion's contrary is always particular; the contradictory is always universal.
3
Test the contrary: why it fails to refute
Take the contrary of the conclusion (a particular proposition) and try to combine it with the minor premise in the first figure, or with the major in the second. For a syllogism to be productive in those figures, the major premise must be universal. But when the contrary is particular, the resulting major premise becomes particular, blocking production. Thus, no refutation is possible.
First and second figures require a universal major; the contrary forces a particular major.
4
Test the contradictory: why it succeeds
The contradictory is universal. If both original premises are universal, the contradictory with the minor (using first figure) yields a contrary to the major; with the major (also first figure) yields a contrary to the minor. If one original premise is particular, the contradictory combines with the minor in the first figure to produce a contradictory to the major, and with the major in the second figure to produce a contradictory to the minor. Hence, the contradictory always yields a valid refutation.
The universal nature of the contradictory allows it to respect the rules of the first and second figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما في الشكل الثالث فمقابل النتيجة بالضد لا يُبطل شيئًا من مقدمتي القياس فإن ضد نتيجة هذا الشكل جزئي. وانتظامه مع الصغرى على هيئة الشكل الأول ، ومع الكبرى على هيئة الشكل الثاني وكيف كان تكون الكبرى جزئية؛ وذلك مانع عن الإنتاج في هذين الشكلين وأما إذا أخذ مقابل النتيجة بالنقيض كان كليًّا لا محالة : فإن كانت الكبرى سالبة كان النقيض موجبا كليًّا، وإن كانت موجبة كان سالبًا كليا وكيف كان؛ فإن كانت المقدمتان كليتين ينتج النقيض مع الصغرى من الشكل الأول ما يضاد الكبرى، ومع الكبرى من الأول أيضًا ما يضاد الصغرى لما أن النتيجة كلية حينئذ. وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين جزئية ينتج النقيض مع الصغرى على هيئة الشكل الأول ما يناقض الكبرى، ومع الكبرى على هيئة الشكل الثاني ما يناقض الصغرى.
As for the third figure, the opposite of the conclusion by contrary does not refute anything of the two premises of the syllogism, because the contrary of the conclusion of this figure is particular. Its arrangement with the minor in the form of the first figure, and with the major in the form of the second figure, and in any case the major becomes particular; and that prevents production in these two figures. As for when one takes the opposite of the conclusion by contradictory, it is necessarily universal: if the major is negative, the contradictory is universal affirmative; if affirmative, it is universal negative, and so on. If both premises are universal, the contradictory together with the minor produces from the first figure what is contrary to the major, and together with the major from the first figure also what is contrary to the minor, because the conclusion then is universal. If one of the premises is particular, the contradictory with the minor in the form of the first figure produces what contradicts the major, and with the major in the form of the second figure what contradicts the minor.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage lays down a general rule for the third figure. The author explains that the contrary (ضد) of the conclusion cannot be used to refute any premise because the contrary of a particular proposition is itself particular. When you try to combine this particular contrary with the minor in the first figure, or with the major in the second figure, the major premise becomes particular, and both the first and second figures require a universal major to produce a conclusion. Therefore, no syllogism results. On the other hand, the contradictory (نقيض) of the conclusion is always universal. The author then details two cases: when both original premises are universal, the contradictory yields a contrary to the other premise; when one premise is particular, the contradictory yields a contradictory. This systematic treatment shows the logic behind valid refutations in dialectical reasoning.

Key Terms
ضد (ḍidd): The contrary of the conclusion; in the third figure, it is always particular because the conclusion is particular.
نقيض (naqīḍ): The contradictory of the conclusion; it is always universal, either universal affirmative or universal negative.
كلية (kulliyya): A universal proposition; in this context, the contradictory is universal, but the contrary is particular.
جزئية (juz'iyya): A particular proposition; the contrary of the third figure conclusion is particular, which prevents production in first and second figures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it is affirmative.
D) Because it is negative.

Key Takeaway
In the third figure, refutation of a premise requires assuming the contradictory (universal) of the conclusion, not the contrary (particular), because only the contradictory yields a productive syllogism.

Refutation in the Fourth Figure: Contrary and Contradictory
Author explains refutation methods in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which form of refutation (contradictory or contrary) is valid for a given fourth-figure categorical syllogism and identify the conditions under which each succeeds or fails.

Core Idea
Refuting a fourth-figure syllogism requires careful mood analysis: contradictory often works, contrary only when the conclusion is universal negative.

This lesson examines how to refute a fourth-figure syllogism by taking the opposite of its conclusion. The author walks through three different premise combinations to show when the contradictory (naqid) refutes the syllogism and when the contrary (didd) fails due to structural restrictions of the figure. You will learn to apply these tests to any fourth-figure argument you encounter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
For each mood, determine whether the conclusion is universal or particular, affirmative or negative. This dictates which opposite (contrary or contradictory) is possible.
Contrary is a universal opposed to a universal; for particular conclusions, the 'contrary' is often a particular negative, but here it's treated as the opposite of the same quantity.
2
Test the contradictory refutation
Take the contradictory of the conclusion (opposite in quality and quantity) and combine it with the major premise. Check if the resulting syllogism is valid and produces a conclusion that contradicts the minor premise. The author shows this works for the first two moods.
Contradictory of 'some C is A' is 'no C is A'; of 'no C is A' is 'some C is A'.
3
Test the contrary refutation
Take the contrary of the conclusion (opposite in quality but same quantity? Actually contrary of universal is universal, of particular is particular) and combine with the major premise. See if the resulting syllogism is structurally valid. The author explains that the particular negative is prohibited in the fourth figure, causing the contrary to fail for particular affirmative conclusions.
Contrary of 'every B is C' is 'no B is C'; contrary of 'some C is A' is 'some C is not A' which is particular negative.
4
Apply to the third mood
When the conclusion is 'no C is A' (universal negative), the contrary is 'some C is A' (particular affirmative). This time the contrary works when combined with the major in the first figure, producing a conclusion that refutes the minor. The contradictory also works but is not fully explained due to the missing text.
This shows that the success of the contrary depends on the mood.

Commentary
This passage examines the fourth figure. The author first considers a universal affirmative minor and universal affirmative major, yielding a particular affirmative conclusion. Taking the contradictory (which would be a universal negative) combines with the major in the fourth mood (i.e., fourth figure premise combination) and yields something that converts to a contrary of the major. The contrary fails because the particular negative is not allowed in the fourth figure. Then he considers a universal affirmative minor and particular affirmative major, where the contradictory works to contradict the minor, but the contrary fails due to two particulars or a particular major in first figure. Next, a universal negative minor and universal affirmative major yields a universal negative conclusion; here the contrary (which would be a particular affirmative) can work by combining with the major in first figure and with minor in fourth mood. The passage ends mid-explanation of the case when taking the contradictory. This shows the complexity of refutation in the fourth figure.

Refutation in the Fourth Figure: Contrapositive vs Opposite
Author examines refutation of premises in fourth figure using contrapositive and opposite conversions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between using the contrapositive and the opposite of a conclusion to refute a premise in the fourth figure, and identify which combinations yield a valid refutation.

Core Idea
Contrapositive refutes; opposite often fails due to figure restrictions on particular negatives.

In the fourth figure of categorical syllogism, refuting a premise requires careful manipulation of the conclusion. The author examines two operations: taking the contrapositive (contradictory) or the opposite (contrary) of the conclusion and combining it with a premise to generate a new syllogism. Understanding when each works is crucial for constructing valid objections in logical debate, especially in Islamic theological and legal reasoning where refutation of premises is common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion of the initial fourth figure syllogism
Given a valid fourth figure syllogism, note the major and minor premises and the conclusion. For example, 'Every B is C' and 'Every A is B' yield 'Some C is A'. This is the starting point for refutation.
The fourth figure has distinct valid moods; this example is a universal affirmative pair concluding particularly.
2
Form the contrapositive and opposite of the conclusion
The contrapositive (المقابل بالنقيض) is the contradictory opposite: for 'Some C is A', the contradictory is 'No C is A'. The opposite (المقابل بالضد) is the contrary: for 'Some C is A', the contrary is 'Not every C is A' (i.e., some C is not A). Note: for universal conclusions, the contrary is the universal negative.
The author uses 'muqabil bi-l-naqid' and 'muqabil bi-l-didd' consistently with Aristotelian square of opposition.
3
Combine the contrapositive or opposite with a premise to form a new syllogism
Take the contrapositive ('No C is A') and combine it with the major premise ('Every A is B') in the fourth mood of the fourth figure (per the author). The resulting conclusion, after conversion, should contradict the targeted premise. For the opposite ('Not every C is A'), it often fails because it would require an invalid mood (e.g., particular negative in fourth figure).
The author refers to specific moods (الضرب الرابع, الخامس) that require knowledge of valid fourth figure moods.
4
Check if the conversion of the new conclusion contradicts the targeted premise
When the contrapositive succeeds, the new syllogism yields a conclusion that, when converted (by simple or accidental conversion), becomes the contradictory of the premise you wish to refute. For example, if the new conclusion converts to 'No B is C' and the original minor premise is 'Every B is C', then the minor is refuted.
If no valid conversion leads to contradiction, the refutation fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في الشكل الرابع فنقول كل ب ج، وكل أ ب فبعض ج أ، فإذا أخذ المقابل بالنقيض ينتظم مع الكبرى على هيئة الضرب الرابع من هذا الشكل، وينتج ما ينعكس إلى ما يضاد الكبرى. وإذا أخذ المقابل بالضد فلا يبطل شيئًا من مقدمتي القياس؛ وذلك لأن انتظامه مع الكبرى على هيئة الرابع؛ والسالبة الجزئية متقاعدة في هذا الشكل، ومع الصغرى على هيئة الشكل الأول؛ والكبرى الجزئية متقاعدة فيه على ما عرف. أو نقول كل ب ج، وبعض أ ب فبعض ج أ، فإذا أخذ المقابل بالنقيض ينتظم مع الكبرى على هيئة الضرب الخامس من هذا الشكل وينتج ما يناقض الصغرى، ومع الصغرى على هيئة الشكل الأول وينتج ما ينعكس إلى ما يناقض الكبرى. وأما إذا أخذ المقابل بالضد فإنه لا يبطل شيئًا أصلا لعدم انتظامه مع الكبرى؛ وقد لا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع الصغرى كذلك؛ وقد كان من اللوازم أن تكون الكبرى كلية في الشكل الأول أو نقول لا شيء من ب ج، وكل أ ب فلا شيء من ج أ، فإذا أخذ المقابل بالضد ينتظم مع الكبرى على هيئة الشكل الأول وينتج ما ينعكس إلى ما يناقض الصغرى، ومع الصغرى على هيئة الضرب الرابع من هذا الشكل وينتج ما يناقض الكبرى. وكذلك إذا أخذ المقابل بالنقيض لكن انتظامه مع الصغرى على هيئة الضرب الخامس من هذا الشكل. أو نقول كل ب ج، ولا شيء من أ ب فليس كل ج أ، فإذا أخذ المقابل بالنقيض ينتظم مع الكبرى على هيئة الشكل الأول وينتج ما ينعكس إلى ما يضاد الصغرى، ومع الصغرى على هيئة الضرب الأول من هذا الشكل وينتج ما يناقض الكبرى. وينتظم على هيئة الشكل الأول أيضًا وينتج ما ينعكس إلى ما يناقض الكبرى. وإذا أخذ المقابل بالضد فذلك لا يبطل شيئًا من المقدمتين؛ أما الكبرى فلعدم انتظامه مع الصغرى، وأما الصغرى فلأنه ينتظم مع الكبرى على هيئة الشكل الأول وينتج بعض ج ليس ب؛ وذلك لا ينعكس إلى ما يناقض الصغرى. أو نقول بعض أ ب ج، ولا شيء من أ ب فبعض ج ليس أ، فإذا أخذ مقابل النتيجة بالنقيض وأضيف إلى الكبرى ينتج من الشكل الأول ما ينعكس إلى ما يناقض الصغرى. وإذا أضيف إلى الصغرى ينتج من الأول أيضًا ما ينعكس إلى ما يناقض الكبرى. والمقابل بالضد لا ينتظم مع الصغرى؛ إذ لا قياس عن جزئيتين، ومع الكبرى ينتج من الشكل الأول ليس بعض ج ب؛ وذلك لا يبطل الصغرى.
As for the fourth figure, we say: Every B is C, and every A is B, so some C is A. If the contrapositive is taken, it combines with the major premise in the fourth mood of this figure and produces something that converts to what contradicts the major. If the opposite is taken, it does not refute either premise because its combination with the major is in the fourth mood, and the particular negative is not allowed in this figure, and with the minor it is in the first figure, and the particular major is not allowed in it as known. Or we say: Every B is C, and some A is B, so some C is A. If the contrapositive is taken, it combines with the major in the fifth mood of this figure and produces what contradicts the minor, and with the minor in the first figure and produces what converts to what contradicts the major. If the opposite is taken, it does not refute anything at all because it does not combine with the major, and there is no syllogism from two particulars, and similarly with the minor; and it was necessary that the major be universal in the first figure. Or we say: No B is C, and every A is B, so no C is A. If the opposite is taken, it combines with the major in the first figure and produces what converts to what contradicts the minor, and with the minor in the fourth mood of this figure and produces what contradicts the major. Similarly if the contrapositive is taken, but its combination with the minor is in the fifth mood of this figure. Or we say: Every B is C, and no A is B, so not every C is A. If the contrapositive is taken, it combines with the major in the first figure and produces what converts to what opposes the minor, and with the minor in the first mood of this figure and produces what contradicts the major. And it also combines in the first figure and produces what converts to what contradicts the major. If the opposite is taken, it does not refute either premise: as for the major, because it does not combine with the minor; as for the minor, because it combines with the major in the first figure and produces some C is not B, and that does not convert to what contradicts the minor. Or we say: Some A is B is C, and no A is B, so some C is not A. If the contrapositive of the conclusion is taken and added to the major, it produces from the first figure what converts to what contradicts the minor. If added to the minor, it also produces from the first figure what converts to what contradicts the major. The opposite does not combine with the minor, since there is no syllogism from two particulars, and with the major it produces from the first figure not some C is B, and that does not refute the minor.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed examination of refutation methods in the fourth figure of categorical syllogism. The author systematically presents different premise combinations (e.g., universal affirmative major and minor, universal major and particular minor, etc.) and shows how taking the contrapositive (المناقض) or opposite (الضد) of the conclusion can be used to refute either the major or minor premise by converting the result to a contradictory proposition. The author notes instances where no refutation is possible, such as when the combination would require a particular negative in the fourth figure (which is invalid) or when the premises are both particular. Throughout, the author refers to specific moods (الضرب) of the fourth figure (e.g., fourth, fifth, first) and invokes the first figure for conversions. The discussion assumes familiarity with the valid moods of each figure. For example, in the first mood examined (كل ب ج، وكل أ ب), the contrapositive combines with the major in the fourth mood and yields a conclusion that converts to contradict the major; the opposite fails because it would require a particular negative. In contrast, for the universal negative premises (لا شيء من ب ج، وكل أ ب), the opposite successfully refutes the minor by converting to its contradictory. The author carefully distinguishes when the contrapositive or opposite works, emphasizing that some combinations are sterile due to figure constraints.

Moods and Refutation: Detailed Analysis of Fourth Figure Cases
Author examines refutation of premises in fourth figure using contrapositive and opposite conversions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze each of the fourth figure premise combinations presented by the author and determine which refutation method (contrapositive or opposite) is effective for refuting the major or minor premise.

Core Idea
Each mood in the fourth figure has different refutation possibilities; universal negatives allow the opposite to work.

The author provides multiple premise combinations in the fourth figure, each with its own refutation profile. By working through these cases, you will internalize the conditions under which the contrapositive or opposite of the conclusion can be used to refute a premise. This prepares you for real debate scenarios where you must choose the correct refutation strategy.

Guided Path
1
Examine the first case: universal affirmative premises (AAI)
Premises: 'Every B is C' and 'Every A is B' => conclusion 'Some C is A'. The contrapositive (No C is A) combines with the major in the fourth mood to produce a conclusion that converts to contradict the major. The opposite fails because it would require a particular negative premise in the fourth figure.
This case shows the importance of avoiding invalid moods in refutation attempts.
2
Examine the second case: universal major, particular minor (IAI)
Premises: 'Every B is C' and 'Some A is B' => conclusion 'Some C is A'. The contrapositive combines with the major in the fifth mood to produce something that contradicts the minor; with the minor in the first figure to produce something that converts to contradict the major. The opposite fails entirely.
The contrapositive can refute either premise depending on which premise it is combined with.
3
Examine the third case: universal negative major, universal affirmative minor (AEE)
Premises: 'No B is C' and 'Every A is B' => conclusion 'No C is A'. Here the opposite (contrary: 'Some C is A') combines with the major in the first figure to produce a conclusion that converts to contradict the minor; with the minor in the fourth mood to produce a conclusion that contradicts the major. Similarly, the contrapositive works but in a different mood.
For universal negative conclusions, the opposite (contrary) is particularly effective.
4
Examine the fourth case: universal affirmative major, universal negative minor (AEO)
Premises: 'Every B is C' and 'No A is B' => conclusion 'Not every C is A' (i.e., some C is not A). The contrapositive (Every C is A) combines with the major in the first figure to produce a conclusion that converts to oppose the minor; with the minor in the first mood of the fourth figure to contradict the major. The opposite fails because it does not produce a contradicting conversion.
The contrapositive succeeds here, while the opposite is ineffective.
5
Examine the fifth case: particular major, universal negative minor (EIO)
Premises: 'Some A is B is C' (i.e., Some A is B and B is C? Actually the text says 'بعض أ ب ج' meaning some A is B and B is C? The given translation: 'Some A is B is C' might be a misprint; it likely means 'Some A is B and every B is C'? The text: 'بعض أ ب ج' could be 'Some A is B and B is C'? But the conclusion is 'بعض ج ليس أ' (Some C is not A). The contrapositive of the conclusion (Every C is A) combined with the major yields a refutation of the minor; combined with the minor yields a refutation of the major. The opposite fails due to two particulars.
This case illustrates the importance of avoiding two-particular syllogisms.

Refuting Syllogism Premises by Conversion Across Figures
Author summarizes which premise is refuted by conversion to which figure for all four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise of a given syllogism figure is refuted by conversion to which other figure, using the rules enumerated in the passage.

Core Idea
Refutation by conversion follows a systematic pattern varying by figure and mood.

In the previous sections, the author explained how to refute a syllogistic premise by converting the conclusion to another figure. Here, he provides a concise summary for all four figures. Mastering this summary allows you to quickly determine the correct refutation strategy for any categorical syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the technique of refutation by conversion
When a syllogism is used as a proof (e.g., for a theological claim), an opponent may attack one of its premises. One method is to convert the conclusion (in'ikās al-qiyās) to produce a new syllogism that contradicts the targeted premise. The passage tells us exactly which premise of which figure is refuted by converting to which figure.
This is a meta-level rule: it describes a strategy, not a single syllogism.
2
Parse the enumeration for the first and third figures
Read the points for the first figure: the minor premise is refuted by conversion to the second figure; the major is refuted by conversion to the first itself and also by conversion to the third. For the third figure: minor refuted by conversion to second; major by conversion to first.
The major of the first figure has two methods: by conversion to first and to third.
3
Parse the enumeration for the fourth figure
The fourth figure has five moods. The author splits them: moods 1-2: minor refuted by conversion to fourth itself, major by conversion to first. Moods 3-4: minor by conversion to first, major by conversion to fourth itself. Mood 5: any conversion goes to first.
The variation shows that the refutation method depends on the mood, not just the figure.
4
Connect refutation by conversion to reductio ad absurdum
The author warns that any objections (shubah) that apply to reductio (khalaf) also apply to this method. This means that the refutation by conversion is logically equivalent to reductio, so any critique of one applies to the other.
This insight helps evaluate the robustness of the method in theological debates.

وبالجملة فقد ظهر بجميع ما ذكرناه [١] أن إبطال صغرى الأول بالانعكاس إلى الثاني [٢] وإبطال كبراه بالانعكاس إلى الأول، [٣] وإبطال كبراه بالانعكاس إلى الثالث [٤] وإبطال صغرى الثالث بالانعكاس إلى الثاني [0] وإبطال كبراه بالانعكاس إلى الأول، [٦] وإبطال صغرى الرابع في الأول والثاني من الضروب بالانعكاس إليه بعينه [۷] وإبطال الكبرى بالانعكاس إلى الأول، [۸] وفي الثالث والرابع إبطال الصغرى بالانعكاس إلى الأول، [٩] وإبطال الكبرى بالانعكاس إليه بعينه [١٠] وفي الخامس كيف كان؛ كان انعكاسه إلى الأول. واعلم بأن ما يرد على الخلف من الشبه فذلك من جملة ما يرد على عكس القياس أيضًا.
In summary, it has become clear from all that we have mentioned: [1] That refuting the minor of the first figure is by conversion to the second. [2] And refuting its major is by conversion to the first. [3] And refuting its major is by conversion to the third. [4] And refuting the minor of the third is by conversion to the second. [0] And refuting its major is by conversion to the first. [6] And refuting the minor of the fourth in the first and second moods is by conversion to itself. [7] And refuting the major is by conversion to the first. [8] And in the third and fourth, refuting the minor is by conversion to the first. [9] And refuting the major is by conversion to itself. [10] And in the fifth, however it is, its conversion is to the first. And know that whatever objections (shubah) arise against reductio ad absurdum (khalaf), they also arise against conversion of the syllogism (aks al-qiyas).

Commentary
This passage is a concise summary of how to refute the premises of syllogisms in each figure by converting the conclusion to another figure. The author lists ten points, each specifying which premise of which figure is refuted by conversion to which figure. The points are numbered in the text, but note that the numbering jumps from [٤] to [0] (likely a misprint for [٥]) and continues. The first few points: the minor premise of the first figure is refuted by converting to the second figure; the major of the first is refuted by converting to the first itself (i.e., using the same figure); the major of the first is also refutable by conversion to the third. For the third figure, its minor is refuted by conversion to the second, and its major by conversion to the first. For the fourth figure, the procedure varies by mood: in the first and second moods, the minor is refuted by conversion to the fourth itself; the major is refuted by conversion to the first. In the third and fourth moods, the minor is refuted by conversion to the first, and the major by conversion to the fourth itself. In the fifth mood, any conversion goes to the first figure. The author concludes by noting that any objections that apply to reductio ad absurdum also apply to this 'conversion of the syllogism' ('aks al-qiyas), implying that the method is susceptible to similar sophistical challenges.

Invalidating Premises via Khulf (Reduction to Absurdity)
Khulf method using contradictory and contrary opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of khulf to invalidate a premise in a categorical syllogism and determine whether the contradictory or contrary opposite is appropriate.

Core Idea
Khulf invalidates premises by assuming the opposite of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction.

This passage from the logic textbook introduces a powerful technique used in Islamic dialectics: reduction to absurdity (khulf). Instead of directly attacking a premise, you take the contradictory of the conclusion and combine it with one premise to derive a statement that contradicts the other premise. This method is essential for testing the validity of syllogisms in theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target syllogism
Work with the author's example: minor 'Every B is C', major 'No A is B', conclusion 'Not every C is A'. Note the terms and their arrangement.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
The conclusion 'Not every C is A' has contradictory 'Every C is A'. Take this as a new premise. This assumption is the key to refutation.
The contrary opposite 'No C is A' does not lead to invalidation.
3
Combine with the other premise in first figure
To invalidate the minor, combine 'Every C is A' with the major 'No A is B' in first figure. This yields a new syllogism: 'Every C is A, No A is B, therefore No C is B'. Convert 'No C is B' to 'No B is C', which is contrary to the minor 'Every B is C'.
The passage notes that such conversion does not truly invalidate the minor because it yields only a contrary, not a contradictory. Only the contradictory opposite leads to a real refutation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Structure of Circular Syllogism
Defining circular syllogism with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid first-figure circular syllogism and identify its parts.

Core Idea
Circular syllogism uses the conclusion and the converse of one premise to derive the other premise.

In dialectical debates, you may need to prove a premise from the conclusion. Circular syllogism (qiyas al-dawr) does exactly that, as the author explains. It is not a fallacy but a valid dialectical tool. Let us walk through its structure using the example given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Locate the original syllogism whose premises you want to derive. In the example: 'Every human is thinking, every thinking is laughing, therefore every human is laughing.'
The conclusion is 'Every human is laughing'.
2
Choose the premise to derive
Decide which original premise you want to prove from the conclusion and the converse of the other premise. Here we derive the minor 'Every human is thinking'.
3
Take the converse of the opposite premise
Convert the major premise 'Every thinking is laughing' to its converse: 'Every laughing is thinking'. Note that the quantity is preserved (universal to universal).
For affirmative universal propositions, the converse is also universal if the terms are reciprocal.
4
Form the new syllogism
Combine the conclusion 'Every human is laughing' (as minor premise) with the converse of the major 'Every laughing is thinking' (as major premise). The conclusion of this new syllogism is the desired premise: 'Every human is thinking'.
This is a Barbara syllogism: All laughing is thinking, all humans are laughing, therefore all humans are thinking.
5
Verify the derivation
Check that the new conclusion exactly matches the original minor premise. Similarly, you can derive the major premise by using the converse of the minor.
This process requires the terms to be reciprocal, as explained in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في قياس الدور] قياس الدور قياس ألف من نتيجة قياس مع عكس إحدى مقدمتيه لينتج المقدمة الأخرى فتارة تكون المقدّمة مؤثرة في وجود النتيجة، وتارة تكون النتيجة مؤثرة في وجود المقدمة. ولا يستراب في أن قياس الدور في مقابلة عكس القياس من حيث إن الدور بصحة النتيجة يصحح المقدمة، والعكس ببطلان النتيجة يُبْطِلُ المقدمة، وكما أن عكس القياس يستعمل في الجدل فكذلك الدور بأن يتلفظ فيعبر المطلوب في اللفظ عن صورته وهو المطلوب ليوهم شيئًا آخر غير المطلوب. وكما أنه يستعمل في الجدل فكذلك في الغير من العلوم؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد. ثم من الشرائط أن في الدور لا تكون الكمية متغيرة في العكس؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن تكون الحدود في المقدمات متعاكسة متساوية بعكس كل واحد منها على الآخر كما في قولنا كل إنسان متفكر، وكل متفكر ضحاك، فتؤخذ النتيجة مع عكس الكبرى ويقال كل إنسان ضحاك، وكل ضحاك متفكر فكل إنسان متفكر. وتؤخذ مع عكس الصغرى / ويقال كل متفكر إنسان، وكل إنسان ضحاك فكل متفكر ضحاك ينتج الأول منهما الصغرى، والثاني الكبرى لما مرّ من قبل غير مرة. وأما الضرب الثاني من الشكل الأول فيضاف عكس الصغرى الموجبة إلى النتيجة لينتج الكبرى السالبة. ولا يضاف عكس الكبرى السالبة إلى النتيجة؛ لما أنه ينتظم من السالبتين حينئذ؛ وذلك لا ينتج أصلا. اللهم إلا إذا كانت السالبة من جنس ما يكون المسلوب خاص السلب عن الموضوع فيكون موجودا في كل ما ليس موصوفًا بالموضوع كما أن العكس في الإيجاب إنما يكون بحيث يكون الإيجاب خاصا بالموضوع فيكون
Chapter on Circular Syllogism. The circular syllogism is a syllogism composed of the conclusion of a syllogism along with the converse of one of its premises, to produce the other premise. Sometimes the premise is effective in the existence of the conclusion, and sometimes the conclusion is effective in the existence of the premise. There is no doubt that circular syllogism is opposite to conversion of the syllogism, in that the circle, by the validity of the conclusion, validates the premise, while conversion, by the invalidity of the conclusion, invalidates the premise. And just as conversion of the syllogism is used in dialectic, so too is the circle, by uttering and expressing the desired thing in speech in its form, which is the desired, to give the impression of something other than the desired. And just as it is used in dialectic, so also in other sciences; that will be known later. Then, among the conditions, in the circle the quantity must not change in the conversion; that is only possible when the terms in the premises are reciprocal and equal, with each one converting onto the other, as in our saying: Every human is thinking, and every thinking is laughing. Then the conclusion is taken with the converse of the major and it is said: Every human is laughing, and every laughing is thinking, so every human is thinking. And it is taken with the converse of the minor and it is said: Every thinking is human, and every human is laughing, so every thinking is laughing. The first of these produces the minor, and the second produces the major, as has been mentioned many times before. As for the second mood of the first figure, the converse of the affirmative minor is added to the conclusion to produce the negative major. And the converse of the negative major is not added to the conclusion, because then it would consist of two negatives, which produces nothing at all. Unless the negative is of the kind where the negated is specifically negated of the subject, so that it exists in everything that is not described by the subject, just as the converse in the affirmative is only such that the affirmation is specific to the subject, so that it is...
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the circular syllogism (qiyas al-dawr). The author first defines it: it is a syllogism formed from the conclusion of another syllogism together with the converse (aks) of one of its premises, in order to derive the other premise. This creates a circle where either the premise validates the conclusion or vice versa. The author distinguishes this from conversion of the syllogism (aks al-qiyas), which invalidates a premise from the invalidity of the conclusion, whereas circular syllogism validates a premise from the validity of the conclusion. The author notes that circular syllogism is used in dialectic (jadal) to create an illusion of proving something else, and also in other sciences. Then the author gives a condition: in a circular syllogism, the quantity (universal/particular) must not change in the conversion. This requires that the terms in the premises are reciprocal (mustawiya), meaning each converts onto the other. An example is given: 'Every human is thinking, every thinking is laughing.' The conclusion is 'Every human is laughing.' By taking this conclusion with the converse of the major ('Every laughing is thinking'), we derive 'Every human is thinking' (the original minor). Similarly, with the converse of the minor ('Every thinking is human'), we derive 'Every thinking is laughing' (the original major). Then the author discusses the second mood of the first figure, where only the converse of the affirmative minor can be used to produce the negative major, because using the converse of the negative major would result in two negative premises, which yield no conclusion. An exception is noted for a special type of negative where the negation is specific. The passage underscores the technical conditions required for a valid circular argument.

Circular syllogism is a valid dialectical method that preserves quantity in conversion.

Conditions of Circular Syllogism: Quantity and the Second Mood
Explaining conditions for valid circular syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a circular syllogism fails due to quantity change or use of two negative premises.

Core Idea
A circular syllogism fails if quantity changes or if two negatives appear.

The author specifies crucial conditions for a valid circular syllogism. The terms must be reciprocal and quantity must be preserved. Moreover, in the second mood of the first figure, only the converse of the affirmative minor may be used; adding the converse of the negative major creates two negatives and no conclusion. Let's examine these conditions closely.

Guided Path
1
Check reciprocity of terms
For a valid circular syllogism, the terms must be 'reciprocal' (mustawiya), meaning each term's converse is equivalent. In the example, 'human' and 'thinking' are reciprocal: every human is thinking and every thinking is human? Actually in the example, the original syllogism is 'Every human is thinking, every thinking is laughing'. Here 'human' and 'thinking' are not necessarily reciprocal (not every thinking is human). Wait, the author says the terms must be 'reciprocal and equal'. Let's re-examine: the original major is 'Every thinking is laughing' and minor 'Every human is thinking'. The converse of the major 'Every laughing is thinking' is true? Not necessarily. But the author assumes it for the sake of the example. So we need to ensure that the converse of each premise holds. In the example, 'Every thinking is laughing' converts to 'Every laughing is thinking' only if the terms are reciprocal. So condition: the premises must be such that their converses are true.
This is a strong condition; not all syllogisms satisfy it.
2
Preserve quantity in conversion
The quantity (universal/particular) must not change in the conversion. For example, 'Every thinking is laughing' (universal) converts to 'Every laughing is thinking' (universal) only if the terms are reciprocal. If the original had 'Some thinking is laughing' (particular), the converse would be 'Some laughing is thinking' (particular). Quantity change would invalidate the circle.
This condition ensures the terms are convertible without loss of generality.
3
Handle the second mood of the first figure
The second mood (i.e., Celarent: no humans are stones, all humans are animals, therefore no animals are stones?) Actually the standard second mood of the first figure: No M is P, All S is M, therefore No S is P. For a circular derivation, the author says: add the converse of the affirmative minor (the minor is 'All S is M', its converse is 'Some M is S') to the conclusion to produce the negative major. Example: from 'No human is stone' and 'All humans are animals' (original minor), you cannot get 'No animal is stone' by circular syllogism because the converse of the major (negative) would create two negatives. So you use the conclusion and the converse of the minor to derive the major.
The converse of the negative major is not added because two negative premises yield no conclusion.
4
Understand the exception for specific negation
The author mentions an exception: if the negative is 'specific negation' (salba khassa), meaning the predicate is denied of the subject in a way that it holds of everything not described by the subject, then the rule may apply differently. This is a nuanced case not fully elaborated here.
This exception is advanced; you may revisit it later.

فصل : [ في قياس الدور] قياس الدور قياس ألف من نتيجة قياس مع عكس إحدى مقدمتيه لينتج المقدمة الأخرى فتارة تكون المقدّمة مؤثرة في وجود النتيجة، وتارة تكون النتيجة مؤثرة في وجود المقدمة. ولا يستراب في أن قياس الدور في مقابلة عكس القياس من حيث إن الدور بصحة النتيجة يصحح المقدمة، والعكس ببطلان النتيجة يُبْطِلُ المقدمة، وكما أن عكس القياس يستعمل في الجدل فكذلك الدور بأن يتلفظ فيعبر المطلوب في اللفظ عن صورته وهو المطلوب ليوهم شيئًا آخر غير المطلوب. وكما أنه يستعمل في الجدل فكذلك في الغير من العلوم؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد. ثم من الشرائط أن في الدور لا تكون الكمية متغيرة في العكس؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن تكون الحدود في المقدمات متعاكسة متساوية بعكس كل واحد منها على الآخر كما في قولنا كل إنسان متفكر، وكل متفكر ضحاك، فتؤخذ النتيجة مع عكس الكبرى ويقال كل إنسان ضحاك، وكل ضحاك متفكر فكل إنسان متفكر. وتؤخذ مع عكس الصغرى / ويقال كل متفكر إنسان، وكل إنسان ضحاك فكل متفكر ضحاك ينتج الأول منهما الصغرى، والثاني الكبرى لما مرّ من قبل غير مرة. وأما الضرب الثاني من الشكل الأول فيضاف عكس الصغرى الموجبة إلى النتيجة لينتج الكبرى السالبة. ولا يضاف عكس الكبرى السالبة إلى النتيجة؛ لما أنه ينتظم من السالبتين حينئذ؛ وذلك لا ينتج أصلا. اللهم إلا إذا كانت السالبة من جنس ما يكون المسلوب خاص السلب عن الموضوع فيكون موجودا في كل ما ليس موصوفًا بالموضوع كما أن العكس في الإيجاب إنما يكون بحيث يكون الإيجاب خاصا بالموضوع فيكون
Chapter on Circular Syllogism. The circular syllogism is a syllogism composed of the conclusion of a syllogism along with the converse of one of its premises, to produce the other premise. Sometimes the premise is effective in the existence of the conclusion, and sometimes the conclusion is effective in the existence of the premise. There is no doubt that circular syllogism is opposite to conversion of the syllogism, in that the circle, by the validity of the conclusion, validates the premise, while conversion, by the invalidity of the conclusion, invalidates the premise. And just as conversion of the syllogism is used in dialectic, so too is the circle, by uttering and expressing the desired thing in speech in its form, which is the desired, to give the impression of something other than the desired. And just as it is used in dialectic, so also in other sciences; that will be known later. Then, among the conditions, in the circle the quantity must not change in the conversion; that is only possible when the terms in the premises are reciprocal and equal, with each one converting onto the other, as in our saying: Every human is thinking, and every thinking is laughing. Then the conclusion is taken with the converse of the major and it is said: Every human is laughing, and every laughing is thinking, so every human is thinking. And it is taken with the converse of the minor and it is said: Every thinking is human, and every human is laughing, so every thinking is laughing. The first of these produces the minor, and the second produces the major, as has been mentioned many times before. As for the second mood of the first figure, the converse of the affirmative minor is added to the conclusion to produce the negative major. And the converse of the negative major is not added to the conclusion, because then it would consist of two negatives, which produces nothing at all. Unless the negative is of the kind where the negated is specifically negated of the subject, so that it exists in everything that is not described by the subject, just as the converse in the affirmative is only such that the affirmation is specific to the subject, so that it is...
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the circular syllogism (qiyas al-dawr). The author first defines it: it is a syllogism formed from the conclusion of another syllogism together with the converse (aks) of one of its premises, in order to derive the other premise. This creates a circle where either the premise validates the conclusion or vice versa. The author distinguishes this from conversion of the syllogism (aks al-qiyas), which invalidates a premise from the invalidity of the conclusion, whereas circular syllogism validates a premise from the validity of the conclusion. The author notes that circular syllogism is used in dialectic (jadal) to create an illusion of proving something else, and also in other sciences. Then the author gives a condition: in a circular syllogism, the quantity (universal/particular) must not change in the conversion. This requires that the terms in the premises are reciprocal (mustawiya), meaning each converts onto the other. An example is given: 'Every human is thinking, every thinking is laughing.' The conclusion is 'Every human is laughing.' By taking this conclusion with the converse of the major ('Every laughing is thinking'), we derive 'Every human is thinking' (the original minor). Similarly, with the converse of the minor ('Every thinking is human'), we derive 'Every thinking is laughing' (the original major). Then the author discusses the second mood of the first figure, where only the converse of the affirmative minor can be used to produce the negative major, because using the converse of the negative major would result in two negative premises, which yield no conclusion. An exception is noted for a special type of negative where the negation is specific. The passage underscores the technical conditions required for a valid circular argument.

عكس
السلب الخاص

The Syllogism

Circular Syllogism: Definition and Contrast with Conversion
Author defines circular syllogism and contrasts it with conversion, highlighting its dialectical use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish circular syllogism from conversion of syllogism and identify its dialectical use.

Core Idea
Circular syllogism is a dialectical tool that uses the conclusion to prove a premise.

In this lesson, you will learn about circular syllogism (qiyas al-dawr), a technique used in dialectical reasoning. The author defines it and contrasts it with conversion of syllogism (aks al-qiyas). Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic. It is 'قياس الدور' (circular syllogism).
The term is explicitly introduced in the section heading.
2
Extract the definition components
The definition: a syllogism taken from the conclusion of another syllogism together with the converse of one of its premises to produce the other premise.
Note the two cases: premise causing conclusion or conclusion causing premise.
3
Contrast with conversion of syllogism
Circular syllogism uses the validity of the conclusion to correct the premise, while conversion uses the falsity of the conclusion to invalidate the premise.
They are opposites in function.
4
Recognize its dialectical function
It is used in dialectic (jadal) and other sciences by expressing the sought result in a form that suggests something else.
This shows it is not a fallacy but a legitimate argumentative tool.

فصل : في قياس الدور. قياس الدور قياس مأخوذ من نتيجة قياس مع عكس إحدى مقدمتيه لينتج المقدمة الأخرى. فتارة تكون المقدمة مؤثرة في وجود النتيجة، وتارة تكون النتيجة مؤثرة في وجود المقدمة. ولا يستراب في أن قياس الدور في مقابلة عكس القياس من حيث إن الدور بصحة النتيجة يصحح المقدمة، والعكس ببطلان النتيجة يبطل المقدمة. وكما أن عكس القياس يستعمل في الجدل فكذلك الدور بأن يتلفظ فيعبر المطلوب في اللفظ عن صورته وهو المطلوب ليوهم شيئا آخر غير المطلوب. وكما أنه يستعمل في الجدل فكذلك في الغير من العلوم؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
Section on circular syllogism. Circular syllogism is a syllogism taken from the conclusion of a syllogism together with the converse of one of its premises to produce the other premise. Sometimes the premise is effective in the existence of the conclusion, and sometimes the conclusion is effective in the existence of the premise. There is no doubt that circular syllogism is opposite to conversion of syllogism in that the circularity corrects the premise by the validity of the conclusion, and conversion invalidates the premise by the falsity of the conclusion. And just as conversion of the syllogism is used in dialectic, so too the circular syllogism is used, by pronouncing and expressing the sought result in speech from its form while it is the sought result to suggest something other than the sought result. And as it is used in dialectic, so too in other sciences; and that is known later.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines circular syllogism (qiyas al-dawr). It begins by stating that circular syllogism is derived from the conclusion of a syllogism combined with the converse of one of its premises to produce the other premise. The author explains that sometimes the premise causes the conclusion, and sometimes the conclusion causes the premise. He then contrasts circular syllogism with conversion (aks al-qiyas): circular syllogism uses the validity of the conclusion to prove a premise, while conversion uses the falsity of the conclusion to disprove a premise. The author notes that both types are employed in dialectic (jadal), but circular syllogism is also used in other sciences. The definition is clear and sets the stage for later conditions and examples.

Key Terms
قياس الدور (qiyas al-dawr): A syllogism formed from the conclusion of another syllogism and the converse of one premise to prove the other premise. [Note: No significant shift in meaning across the tradition; it remains a standard logical concept.]
عكس القياس (aks al-qiyas): Conversion of a syllogism, where the falsity of the conclusion is used to disprove a premise. [Note: In earlier texts, 'aks' primarily refers to immediate inference; here it refers to a dialectical technique.]
جدل (jadal): Dialectical reasoning used to test premises or persuade; often involves circular syllogism or conversion. [Note: In later legal theory, jadal becomes a formal method of disputation; here it refers to a general logical practice.]

D) Circular syllogism is always invalid; conversion is always valid.

Key Takeaway
Circular syllogism is a dialectical tool that uses the validity of the conclusion to prove a premise, opposite to conversion which uses falsity to disprove.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Condition for Circular Syllogism: Term Equality and Unchanged Quantity
Author states the condition for circular syllogism: no change in quantity in the converse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given pair of universal affirmative premises satisfies the condition for circular syllogism (coextensive terms and unchanged quantity in conversion).

Core Idea
Circular syllogism requires terms to be coextensive and quantities preserved in conversion.

Before applying circular syllogism, you must first ensure the premises meet a fundamental condition: the terms must be such that when you convert each premise, the quantity does not change. For universal affirmatives, this means the terms are coextensive (every instance of one is an instance of the other). The author illustrates this with the example 'Every human is thinking' and 'Every thinking is laughing', treating them as if coextensive for logical demonstration. Mastering this condition helps you avoid invalid circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Look at the given pair of universal affirmative premises: e.g., Every C is B and Every B is A. Their terms are C, B, and A. Note that the conclusion would be Every C is A.
The example in the text: Every human is thinking, Every thinking is laughing.
2
Check conversion of each premise
Convert each premise. For a universal affirmative, the natural converse is particular affirmative (Every B is A converts to Some A is B). For circular syllogism, we need the converse to also be universal affirmative. This only happens if the terms are coextensive (the set of B equals the set of A). Verify that the converse of each premise is universal, not particular.
In the text, 'Every human is thinking' would naturally convert to 'Some thinking is human', but the author assumes a special case where terms are equal.
3
Confirm term equality
Examine whether the terms are actually coextensive. If the subject and predicate in each premise refer to the same set (e.g., 'human' and 'thinking' are not actually identical, but for demonstration they are treated as such), then the converses will be universal. Otherwise, circularity fails.
In reality, 'human' and 'thinking' are not coextensive, so this example is a logical exercise, not a real-world claim.
4
Apply the condition to prove circularity
If the condition holds, you can proceed: the conclusion plus the converse of the major proves the minor, and the conclusion plus the converse of the minor proves the major. This is the essence of circular syllogism.
This step is a bridge to the next lesson.

ثم من الشرائط أن في الدور لا تكون الكمية متغيرة في العكس؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن تكون الحدود في المقدمات متعاكسة متساوية بعكس كل واحد منها على الآخر كما في قولنا كل إنسان متفكر، وكل متفكر ضحاك، فتؤخذ موضوعا بالموضوع. ومثل هذه السالبة ما يرجع إلى العدول الموجب فينعكس بالإيجاب كما في قولنا لا شيء من الجوهر بعرض فإن هذا السلب مخصوص بالجوهر لما أن كل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض فينعكس إذن إلى قولنا كل ما ليس بعرض فهو جوهر وإلا فبعض ما ليس بعرض ليس بجوهر، وكل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض ينتج بعض ما ليس بعرض فهو عرض وإنه محال. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إذا كان المراد إثبات الموجبة في قولنا كل ج ب، ولا شيء من ب أ، كان من Then among the conditions is that in circular syllogism the quantity does not change in the converse; that is not possible unless the terms in the premises are corresponding and equal by the converse of each one to the other, as in our statement: Every human is thinking, and every thinking is laughing. Then the conclusion is taken together with the converse of the major and it is said: Every human is laughing, and every laughing is thinking, so every human is thinking. And it is taken together with the converse of the minor and it is said: Every thinking is human, and every human is laughing, so every thinking is laughing. The first of these produces the minor, and the second produces the major, as has passed before many times. As for the second mood of the first figure, the converse of the affirmative minor is added to the conclusion to produce the negative major. And the converse of the negative major is not added to the conclusion, because then it would be composed of two negatives, and that yields nothing at all. Unless the negative is of the kind where the negated is a specific negation from the subject, so it exists in everything not described by the subject, just as in affirmative conversion the affirmation is specific to the subject, so it is negated from everything not subject to the subject. An example of such a negative is that which reverts to affirmative by modification (al-‘udūl), as in our statement: No substance is an accident, for this negation is specific to substance because everything that is not a substance is an accident. So it converts to our statement: Everything that is not an accident is a substance. Otherwise, some things that are not accidents are not substances, and everything that is not a substance is an accident, yielding: Some things that are not accidents are accidents, which is impossible. If you know this, we say: If the intention is to prove the affirmative in our statement: Every C is B, and no B is A, then it is necessary to convert the conclusion to the affirmative by modification (al-mujab al-ma‘dul) because their subjects exist, and it is said: Every C is not A, and everything not A is B, to yield: Every C is B. So our statement: Everything not A is B is a consequent of the negative which has passed. As for the third mood, it is obvious because the major cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the minor, since both are particular. As for the minor, it can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, as it is said: Some C is A, and every A is B yields: Some C is B. As for the fourth mood, neither premise can be demonstrated by the conclusion and the converse of the other. As for the major, it is as passed in the third mood, and as for the minor, because the converse of the conclusion is negative, and the negative is not suitable as a minor in the first figure unless the negative is of the kind mentioned. Then the conclusion is converted to the affirmative by modification and added to what is necessitated by the converse of the negative until the identical minor is produced. As for the second figure, the negative premise in the first mood can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the affirmative minor as in our statement: Every C is B, and no A is B, so the major can be proven by the converse with the conclusion, and it is said: Every B is C, and no C is A, so no B is A; and it converts to the negative major.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage explains the technical conditions for applying circular syllogism. It begins by stating that the quantities of terms must remain unchanged in the converse, meaning the terms must be co-extensive (i.e., universal affirmative and convertible). The author illustrates with the first mood of the first figure: using the terms 'human', 'thinking', and 'laughing', he shows how the conclusion with the converse of the major proves the minor, and with the converse of the minor proves the major. For the second mood (universal negative major), only the minor can be proven by adding the converse of the affirmative minor to the conclusion; the converse of the negative major cannot be added because two negatives yield no conclusion, unless the negative is 'specific' such that it behaves like an affirmative by modification (e.g., 'No substance is accident' converts to 'Everything not accident is substance'). For the third mood (particular premises), only the minor can be proven. For the fourth mood (particular major, negative minor), neither premise can be proven directly; the conclusion must be converted to an affirmative by modification and used with a specific converse. Finally, for the second figure, the negative premise in the first mood can be proven by using the converse of the affirmative minor with the conclusion. The author systematically covers each mood, showing the possibilities and limitations of circular syllogism in categorical logic.

Key Terms
العدول
الموجبة المعدولة
العكس

For circular syllogism to work, the terms must be coextensive so that the converses remain universal affirmatives.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
First Figure Mood 1: Proving Both Premises via Circular Syllogism
Author demonstrates circular syllogism in first figure first mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove both the minor and major premises of a first-figure first-mood syllogism using the conclusion and the converse of the other premise.

Core Idea
In the first mood, the conclusion plus the converse of the major proves the minor, and the conclusion plus the converse of the minor proves the major.

The author now shows how the circular syllogism actually works in practice. Using the first mood (Barbara) with the example 'Every human is thinking, every thinking is laughing', he demonstrates two rounds: first, the conclusion 'Every human is laughing' combined with the converse of the major ('Every laughing is thinking') yields the minor premise. Second, the conclusion combined with the converse of the minor ('Every thinking is human') yields the major. This is possible only because the terms are treated as coextensive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Write down the minor premise (Every C is B), major premise (Every B is A), and conclusion (Every C is A). In the example: C=human, B=thinking, A=laughing.
This is a standard first figure, first mood (Barbara) syllogism.
2
Prove the minor using conclusion and converse of major
Take the conclusion (Every C is A) and the converse of the major (Every A is B, assuming coextensiveness). Form a new syllogism: Every C is A, Every A is B, therefore Every C is B. That is the original minor premise.
The author says: 'the conclusion is taken together with the converse of the major and it is said: Every human is laughing, and every laughing is thinking, so every human is thinking.'
3
Prove the major using conclusion and converse of minor
Similarly, take the conclusion (Every C is A) and the converse of the minor (Every B is C). Form: Every C is A, Every B is C cannot directly give Every B is A? Actually read: The author uses 'converse of the minor' with conclusion to prove the major. In his example: 'Every thinking is human, and every human is laughing, so every thinking is laughing' which is the major. So the structure is: conclusion (Every human is laughing) and converse of minor (Every thinking is human) yields major (Every thinking is laughing). So the syllogism is: Every thinking is human, Every human is laughing, therefore Every thinking is laughing. That is a valid first figure syllogism with minor=converse of original minor, major=conclusion, conclusion=original major.
Thus the circular proof requires that the converse of the minor be universal, which holds if terms are coextensive.
4
Verify the coextensiveness condition
For the proofs to work, the converses must be universal. This is only valid if the original terms are coextensive (every C is B and every B is A, plus the converses hold in the same way). If not, the proof fails.
In real Islamic sciences, this condition is rarely met without additional assumptions.
