ثم من الشرائط أن في الدور لا تكون الكمية متغيرة في العكس؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن تكون الحدود في المقدمات متعاكسة متساوية بعكس كل واحد منها على الآخر كما في قولنا كل إنسان متفكر، وكل متفكر ضحاك، فتؤخذ النتيجة مع عكس الكبرى ويقال كل إنسان ضحاك، وكل ضحاك متفكر فكل إنسان متفكر. وتؤخذ مع عكس الصغرى ويقال كل متفكر إنسان، وكل إنسان ضحاك فكل متفكر ضحاك ينتج الأول منهما الصغرى، والثاني الكبرى لما مر من قبل غير مرة. وأما الضرب الثاني من الشكل الأول فيضاف عكس الصغرى الموجبة إلى النتيجة لينتج الكبرى السالبة. ولا يضاف عكس الكبرى السالبة إلى النتيجة؛ لما أنه ينتظم من السالبتين حينئذ؛ وذلك لا ينتج أصلا. اللهم إلا إذا كانت السالبة من جنس ما يكون المسلوب خاص السلب عن الموضوع فيكون موجودا في كل ما ليس موصوفا بالموضوع كما أن العكس في الإيجاب إنما يكون بحيث يكون الإيجاب خاصا بالموضوع فيكون مسلوبا عن كل ما ليس موضوعا بالموضوع. ومثل هذه السالبة ما يرجع إلى العدول الموجب فينعكس بالإيجاب كما في قولنا لا شيء من الجوهر بعرض فإن هذا السلب مخصوص بالجوهر لما أن كل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض فينعكس إذن إلى قولنا كل ما ليس بعرض فهو جوهر وإلا فبعض ما ليس بعرض ليس بجوهر، وكل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض ينتج بعض ما ليس بعرض فهو عرض وإنه محال. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إذا كان المراد إثبات الموجبة في قولنا كل ج ب، ولا شيء من ب أ، كان من اللوازم أن تحول النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة لوجود موضوعهما ويقال كل ج هو ليس أ، وكل ما ليس أ فهو ب لينتج كل ج ب؛ فقولنا وكل ما ليس أ فهو ب من لوازم السالبة التي مر ذكرها. وأما الضرب الثالث منه فظاهر لما أن الكبرى منه لا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى لكونهما جزئيتين فأما الصغرى فيمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى كما يقال بعض ج أ، وكل أ ب ينتج بعض ج ب. وأما الضرب الرابع منه فلا يمكن بيان شيء من مقدمتيه بالنتيجة وعكس الأخرى؛ أما الكبرى فلما مر في الضرب الثالث، وأما الصغرى فلأن عكس النتيجة سالبة والسالبة لا تصلح للصغروية في الشكل الأول إلا إذا كانت السالبة من جنس ما مر ذكره. وحينئذ تحول النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضاف إليها ما يلزم السالبة من العكس حتى ينتج الصغرى بعينها. وأما الشكل الثاني فالمقدمة السالبة في الضرب الأول مما يمكن إثباته بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى الموجبة كما في قولنا كل ج ب، ولا شيء من أ ب، فإنه يمكن إثبات الكبرى بالعكس مع النتيجة فيقال كل ب ج، ولا شيء من ج أ فلا شيء من ب أ؛ وإنه ينعكس إلى الكبرى السالبة وهي
Then among the conditions is that in circular syllogism the quantity does not change in the converse; that is not possible unless the terms in the premises are corresponding and equal by the converse of each one to the other, as in our statement: Every human is thinking, and every thinking is laughing. Then the conclusion is taken together with the converse of the major and it is said: Every human is laughing, and every laughing is thinking, so every human is thinking. And it is taken together with the converse of the minor and it is said: Every thinking is human, and every human is laughing, so every thinking is laughing. The first of these produces the minor, and the second produces the major, as has passed before many times. As for the second mood of the first figure, the converse of the affirmative minor is added to the conclusion to produce the negative major. And the converse of the negative major is not added to the conclusion, because then it would be composed of two negatives, and that yields nothing at all. Unless the negative is of the kind where the negated is a specific negation from the subject, so it exists in everything not described by the subject, just as in affirmative conversion the affirmation is specific to the subject, so it is negated from everything not subject to the subject. An example of such a negative is that which reverts to affirmative by modification (al-‘udūl), as in our statement: No substance is an accident, for this negation is specific to substance because everything that is not a substance is an accident. So it converts to our statement: Everything that is not an accident is a substance. Otherwise, some things that are not accidents are not substances, and everything that is not a substance is an accident, yielding: Some things that are not accidents are accidents, which is impossible. If you know this, we say: If the intention is to prove the affirmative in our statement: Every C is B, and no B is A, then it is necessary to convert the conclusion to the affirmative by modification (al-mujab al-ma‘dul) because their subjects exist, and it is said: Every C is not A, and everything not A is B, to yield: Every C is B. So our statement: Everything not A is B is a consequent of the negative which has passed. As for the third mood, it is obvious because the major cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the minor, since both are particular. As for the minor, it can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, as it is said: Some C is A, and every A is B yields: Some C is B. As for the fourth mood, neither premise can be demonstrated by the conclusion and the converse of the other. As for the major, it is as passed in the third mood, and as for the minor, because the converse of the conclusion is negative, and the negative is not suitable as a minor in the first figure unless the negative is of the kind mentioned. Then the conclusion is converted to the affirmative by modification and added to what is necessitated by the converse of the negative until the identical minor is produced. As for the second figure, the negative premise in the first mood can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the affirmative minor as in our statement: Every C is B, and no A is B, so the major can be proven by the converse with the conclusion, and it is said: Every B is C, and no C is A, so no B is A; and it converts to the negative major.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage explains the technical conditions for applying circular syllogism. It begins by stating that the quantities of terms must remain unchanged in the converse, meaning the terms must be co-extensive (i.e., universal affirmative and convertible). The author illustrates with the first mood of the first figure: using the terms 'human', 'thinking', and 'laughing', he shows how the conclusion with the converse of the major proves the minor, and with the converse of the minor proves the major. For the second mood (universal negative major), only the minor can be proven by adding the converse of the affirmative minor to the conclusion; the converse of the negative major cannot be added because two negatives yield no conclusion, unless the negative is 'specific' such that it behaves like an affirmative by modification (e.g., 'No substance is accident' converts to 'Everything not accident is substance'). For the third mood (particular premises), only the minor can be proven. For the fourth mood (particular major, negative minor), neither premise can be proven directly; the conclusion must be converted to an affirmative by modification and used with a specific converse. Finally, for the second figure, the negative premise in the first mood can be proven by using the converse of the affirmative minor with the conclusion. The author systematically covers each mood, showing the possibilities and limitations of circular syllogism in categorical logic.

Key Terms
العدول
الموجبة المعدولة
العكس

In the first mood circular demonstration, what does the combination of the conclusion and the converse of the major produce?
A) The major premise
B) The minor premise
C) A new conclusion
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
In the first figure first mood, circular syllogism proves the minor via conclusion + converse of major, and the major via conclusion + converse of minor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the syllogism: 'Every halal is permissible' and 'Every permissible is allowed' (treating terms as coextensive for the exercise). Show the circular proof: how would you prove the minor using the conclusion and converse of the major? Write the steps.
?

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
First Figure Mood 2: The Role of Modification (al-‘Adl) for Negative Premises
Author explains the exception for specific negatives and the use of modification in the second mood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a universal negative proposition into an affirmative by modification (al-‘adl) to enable circular proof in the first figure's second mood.

Core Idea
A universal negative can be treated as an affirmative by negating the predicate, allowing circular syllogism where direct conversion fails.

In the second mood (Every C is B, No B is A), the negative major cannot be used directly because adding its converse to the conclusion would give two negatives, yielding no conclusion. The author introduces an exception: if the negative is 'specific', meaning that the negation applies exclusively to the subject, then it can be transformed via modification into a universal affirmative. For example, 'No substance is accident' becomes 'Everything not accident is substance'. This modification (al-'adl) allows the circular proof to proceed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second mood syllogism
Take a second mood of the first figure: minor is universal affirmative (Every C is B), major is universal negative (No B is A). The conclusion is a universal negative (Every C is not A, i.e., No C is A).
Example in text: Every C is B, No B is A.
2
Why direct circular proof fails
To prove the minor, you would try: conclusion (No C is A) and converse of major (No A is B) gives two negatives, which yields no valid syllogism. So a different approach is needed.
The text says: 'the converse of the negative major is not added... because it would be composed of two negatives, and that yields nothing at all.'
3
Recognize a 'specific' negative
Some negatives are such that the negation is specific to the subject: the predicate is negated from the subject in a way that implies the opposite term applies to everything else. For instance, 'No substance is accident' implies 'Everything not substance is accident'. Such negatives can be modified.
The author gives the example: 'No substance is accident' converts via modification to 'Everything not accident is substance'.
4
Perform the modification (al-‘adl)
Convert the negative proposition into an affirmative by negating the predicate while keeping the subject. For 'No B is A', write 'Every B is not A' (which is still negative), then turn it into an affirmative: 'Every B is not-A' becomes a 'mujab ma'dul' (affirmative by modification). The result is an affirmative proposition with a negated predicate, and it can be converted to 'Every not-A is B' (universal affirmative) if the negation is specific.
The text: 'such a negative reverts to the affirmative by modification'.
5
Use the modified converse to complete the circular proof
Now you have a universal affirmative converse of the major (after modification). Combine this with the conclusion to prove the minor. Similarly, you can prove the major by other means.
This step is complex; the author implies it is possible but does not elaborate fully here.

Second Figure Mood 1: Proving the Negative Premise via Circular Syllogism
Author shows how to prove the negative premise in the second figure first mood using circular syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the negative premise in a second-figure first-mood syllogism using the conclusion and the converse of the affirmative minor.

Core Idea
In the second figure, the negative premise can be proven by combining the conclusion with the converse of the affirmative minor, then converting the result.

The author now turns to the second figure. In the first mood (All A is B, No C is B → No C is A), the negative major (No C is B) can be proven using the conclusion and the converse of the affirmative minor. The method: take the conclusion (No C is A) and the converse of the minor (Every B is A? Actually careful: minor is All A is B, its converse is Some B is A, but for circularity we need universal. The author assumes coextensiveness again. The example: Every C is B, No A is B (which is actually a second figure mood? Wait re-read). The author's example: 'Every C is B, and no A is B' is standard Cesare (second figure). The conclusion is No C is A. To prove the negative major (No A is B), he uses the conclusion (No C is A) and the converse of the affirmative minor (Every B is C, assuming coextensiveness) to get No B is A, then converts to No A is B.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure mood
Consider the second figure, first mood: minor is universal affirmative (Every A is B), major is universal negative (No C is B), conclusion is universal negative (No C is A). The author uses a variation: Every C is B (affirmative), No A is B (negative), conclusion No C is A. This is Cesare (second figure).
In Cesare: No A is B, Every C is B → No C is A. Here minor is Every C is B, major No A is B.
2
Select the premise to prove
We aim to prove the negative major (No A is B). In the text, they prove the negative major via the conclusion and the converse of the affirmative minor.
The affirmative minor is 'Every C is B'. Its converse, assuming coextensiveness, is 'Every B is C'.
3
Combine the conclusion with the converse of the minor
Take the conclusion (No C is A) and the converse of the minor (Every B is C). Form a new syllogism: Every B is C, No C is A, therefore No B is A. This is valid (Celarent in first figure).
The text says: 'Every B is C, and no C is A, so no B is A'.
4
Convert the result to the original major
The derived proposition 'No B is A' is the converse of the original major 'No A is B'. Since universal negatives convert simply, you can obtain the original major by conversion.
The text: 'and it converts to the negative major'.

بعرض فهو جوهر وإلا فبعض ما ليس بعرض ليس بجوهر، وكل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض ينتج بعض ما ليس بعرض فهو عرض وإنه محال. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إذا كان المراد إثبات الموجبة في قولنا كل ج ب، ولا شيء من ب أ، كان من اللوازم أن تحول النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة لوجود موضوعهما ويقال كل ج هو ليس أ، وكل ما ليس أ فهو ب لينتج كل ج ب؛ فقولنا وكل ما ليس أ فهو ب من لوازم السالبة التي مر ذكرها. وأما الضرب الثالث منه فظاهر لما أن الكبرى منه لا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى لكونهما جزئيتين فأما الصغرى فيمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى كما يقال بعض ج أ، وكل أ ب ينتج بعض ج ب. وأما الضرب الرابع منه فلا يمكن بيان شيء من مقدمتيه بالنتيجة وعكس الأخرى؛ أما الكبرى فلما مر في الضرب الثالث، وأما الصغرى فلأن عكس النتيجة سالبة والسالبة لا تصلح للصغروية في الشكل الأول إلا إذا كانت السالبة من جنس ما مر ذكره. وحينئذ تحول النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضاف إليها ما يلزم السالبة من العكس حتى ينتج الصغرى بعينها. وأما الشكل الثاني فالمقدمة السالبة في الضرب الأول مما يمكن إثباته بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى الموجبة كما في قولنا كل ج ب، ولا شيء من أ ب، فإنه يمكن إثبات الكبرى بالعكس مع النتيجة فيقال كل ب ج، ولا شيء من ج أ فلا شيء من ب أ؛ وإنه ينعكس إلى الكبرى السالبة وهي
Then among the conditions is that in circular syllogism the quantity does not change in the converse; that is not possible unless the terms in the premises are corresponding and equal by the converse of each one to the other, as in our statement: Every human is thinking, and every thinking is laughing. Then the conclusion is taken together with the converse of the major and it is said: Every human is laughing, and every laughing is thinking, so every human is thinking. And it is taken together with the converse of the minor and it is said: Every thinking is human, and every human is laughing, so every thinking is laughing. The first of these produces the minor, and the second produces the major, as has passed before many times. As for the second mood of the first figure, the converse of the affirmative minor is added to the conclusion to produce the negative major. And the converse of the negative major is not added to the conclusion, because then it would be composed of two negatives, and that yields nothing at all. Unless the negative is of the kind where the negated is a specific negation from the subject, so it exists in everything not described by the subject, just as in affirmative conversion the affirmation is specific to the subject, so it is negated from everything not subject to the subject. An example of such a negative is that which reverts to affirmative by modification (al-‘udūl), as in our statement: No substance is an accident, for this negation is specific to substance because everything that is not a substance is an accident. So it converts to our statement: Everything that is not an accident is a substance. Otherwise, some things that are not accidents are not substances, and everything that is not a substance is an accident, yielding: Some things that are not accidents are accidents, which is impossible. If you know this, we say: If the intention is to prove the affirmative in our statement: Every C is B, and no B is A, then it is necessary to convert the conclusion to the affirmative by modification (al-mujab al-ma‘dul) because their subjects exist, and it is said: Every C is not A, and everything not A is B, to yield: Every C is B. So our statement: Everything not A is B is a consequent of the negative which has passed. As for the third mood, it is obvious because the major cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the minor, since both are particular. As for the minor, it can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, as it is said: Some C is A, and every A is B yields: Some C is B. As for the fourth mood, neither premise can be demonstrated by the conclusion and the converse of the other. As for the major, it is as passed in the third mood, and as for the minor, because the converse of the conclusion is negative, and the negative is not suitable as a minor in the first figure unless the negative is of the kind mentioned. Then the conclusion is converted to the affirmative by modification and added to what is necessitated by the converse of the negative until the identical minor is produced. As for the second figure, the negative premise in the first mood can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the affirmative minor as in our statement: Every C is B, and no A is B, so the major can be proven by the converse with the conclusion, and it is said: Every B is C, and no C is A, so no B is A; and it converts to the negative major.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage explains the technical conditions for applying circular syllogism. It begins by stating that the quantities of terms must remain unchanged in the converse, meaning the terms must be co-extensive (i.e., universal affirmative and convertible). The author illustrates with the first mood of the first figure: using the terms 'human', 'thinking', and 'laughing', he shows how the conclusion with the converse of the major proves the minor, and with the converse of the minor proves the major. For the second mood (universal negative major), only the minor can be proven by adding the converse of the affirmative minor to the conclusion; the converse of the negative major cannot be added because two negatives yield no conclusion, unless the negative is 'specific' such that it behaves like an affirmative by modification (e.g., 'No substance is accident' converts to 'Everything not accident is substance'). For the third mood (particular premises), only the minor can be proven. For the fourth mood (particular major, negative minor), neither premise can be proven directly; the conclusion must be converted to an affirmative by modification and used with a specific converse. Finally, for the second figure, the negative premise in the first mood can be proven by using the converse of the affirmative minor with the conclusion. The author systematically covers each mood, showing the possibilities and limitations of circular syllogism in categorical logic.

Key Terms
العدول
الموجبة المعدولة
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Every B is A
C) No A is B
D) Some B is A

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, the negative premise can be proved by combining the conclusion with the converse of the affirmative minor and then converting the result.

Converting a Special Negative to Modified Affirmative
Author explains conversion of special negative to modified affirmative with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a special negative proposition (saliba makhsusa) into a modified affirmative (mujiba ma'dula) using the necessary consequence principle.

Core Idea
Some negatives hide an affirmative that reveals their true scope.

Not all negative propositions are alike. The author shows that a negative 'private' to its subject can be transformed into a universal affirmative by a clever logical shift, which becomes a key tool for proving syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special negative
Look for a universal negative proposition where the subject term covers exactly the negation of the predicate. Example: 'No substance is an accident' – the subject 'substance' excludes all accidents.
The negative must be 'specific' to the subject, meaning everything not the predicate falls under the subject.
2
Recognize the necessary consequence
From the special negative, derive what is necessarily true: anything that is not the predicate must be the subject. For 'No substance is an accident', the consequence is 'Everything not an accident is a substance'.
This consequence is called lazim al-saliba.
3
Convert to modified affirmative
State the consequence as a universal affirmative with a qualified subject: 'Everything that is not an accident is a substance'. This is the modified affirmative (mujiba ma'dula).
The conversion is valid only if the original negative is special; otherwise it may lead to impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلوبا عن كل ما ليس موضوعا بالموضوع. ومثل هذه السالبة ما يرجع إلى العدول الموجب فينعكس بالإيجاب كما في قولنا لا شيء من الجوهر بعرض فإن هذا السلب مخصوص بالجوهر لما أن كل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض فينعكس إذن إلى قولنا كل ما ليس بعرض فهو جوهر وإلا فبعض ما ليس بعرض ليس بجوهر، وكل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض ينتج بعض ما ليس بعرض فهو عرض ؛ وإنه محال . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إذا كان المراد إثبات الموجبة في قولنا كل ج) ب) ، ولا شيء من ب أن كان من اللوازم أن تحوّل النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة لوجود موضوعهما ويقال كل (ج)) هو ليس «أ»، وكل ما ليس «أ» فهو «ب» لينتج كل (ج) ب»؛ فقولنا وكل ما ليس «أ» فهو «ب» من لوازم السالبة التي مرّ ذكرها. وأما الضرب الثالث منه فظاهر لما أن الكبرى منه لا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى لكونهما جزئيتين فـ . فأما الصغرى فيمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى كما يقال بعض (ج) أ»، وكل «أب ينتج بعض (ج) ب». وأما الضرب الرابع منه فلا يمكن بيان شيء من مقدمتيه بالنتيجة وعكس الأخرى؛ أما الكبرى فلما مرَّ في الضرب الثالث، وأما الصغرى فلأن عكس النتيجة سالبة والسالبة لا تصلح للصغروية في الشكل الأول إلا إذا كانت السالبة من جنس ما مر ذكــره. وحينئذ تحول النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضاف إليها ما يلزم السالبة من العكس حتى ينتج الصغرى بعينها.
Negated of everything that is not a subject by the subject. And an example of such a negative is what reverts to the positive with qualification, so it converts to the positive as in our saying: No substance is an accident. For this negation is specific to substance because everything that is not a substance is an accident, so it converts then to our saying: Everything that is not an accident is a substance. Otherwise, some of what is not an accident is not a substance, and everything that is not a substance is an accident, producing: Some of what is not an accident is an accident; and that is impossible. If you understand this, we say: If the intention is to prove the affirmative in our saying: Every J is B, and No B is A, then it is among the necessary consequences that the conclusion be transformed into the modified affirmative due to the existence of their subjects, and it is said: Every J is not A, and Everything that is not A is B, so that it produces: Every J is B. So our saying: Everything that is not A is B is among the necessary consequences of the negative that has been mentioned previously. As for the third mood of it, it is apparent because the major premise in it cannot be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the minor, because they are both particular. So as for the minor, it can be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the major, as is said: Some J is A, and Every A is B, producing: Some J is B. As for the fourth mood of it, neither of its two premises can be shown by the conclusion and the conversion of the other. As for the major, due to what passed in the third mood; as for the minor, because the conversion of the conclusion is negative, and the negative is not suitable for being the minor in the first figure unless the negative is of the type that was mentioned previously. And then the conclusion is transformed into the modified affirmative, and there is added to it what is necessary for the negative from conversion, until it produces the minor itself.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage explains how to prove the major premise of a syllogism in the first figure using conversion and the special nature of certain negative propositions. The author begins by discussing a type of negative proposition that is specific to a subject and can be converted into a modified affirmative (ma'dula). For example, the proposition 'No substance is an accident' is specific to substance because everything non-substance is an accident. This converts to 'Everything that is not an accident is a substance', but if the conversion were not so specific, it might lead to an impossibility. The author then applies this to proving the conclusion. When we want to prove the major premise 'Every J is B' and the minor is 'No B is A', we transform the conclusion into a modified affirmative: 'Every J is not A', and then use the necessary consequence of the negative: 'Everything that is not A is B', which together produce the desired conclusion. The author then discusses the third and fourth moods of the first figure. In the third mood (both premises particular), the major cannot be proven via conversion, but the minor can be proven by concluding 'Some J is B' and converting the major 'Every B is A'. In the fourth mood, neither premise can be shown directly due to the negative conversion of the conclusion being unsuitable for the minor, unless the negative is of the special type, in which case the conclusion is transformed into a modified affirmative and combined with the necessary consequence to produce the minor.

موجبة معدولة

Proving the Major Premise in First Figure
Author demonstrates how to prove the major premise using modified affirmative and lazim al-saliba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the major premise of a first-figure syllogism when the minor is a universal negative, using the conclusion and the necessary consequence of the minor.

Core Idea
The minor's negative hides an affirmative that completes the proof.

When the minor premise is a universal negative, direct conversion is not possible. However, the author reveals a clever method: transform the conclusion into a modified affirmative and then use the logical shadow of the negative (lazim al-saliba) to connect the terms and prove the original conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism components
We have a first-figure syllogism with conclusion 'Every J is B' and minor premise 'No B is A' (universal negative). The major is the premise we want to prove.
The target is to prove the conclusion using only the other premise and conversion rules.
2
Transform the conclusion into a modified affirmative
Convert the conclusion 'Every J is B' into an equivalent proposition: 'Every J is not A' (since 'not A' is the predicate of the minor's consequence). This is the modified affirmative (mujiba ma'dula).
This step is valid only if we know that A and B are related through the minor.
3
Extract the necessary consequence of the minor
From the minor 'No B is A', derive its necessary consequence: 'Everything not A is B'. This is the lazim al-saliba, a universal affirmative.
The derivation relies on the minor being a special negative or the general principle that from 'No B is A' we can infer 'Everything not A is B' (if the negation is universal and the subject is 'B', but the author assumes a special relationship).
4
Combine the modified conclusion and the lazim
Now we have two premises: 'Every J is not A' (modified conclusion) and 'Everything not A is B' (lazim). From these, using a first-figure syllogism (Every J is not A, Everything not A is B, therefore Every J is B), we prove the original conclusion.
This is a valid Barbara syllogism if we treat 'not A' as the middle term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Limitations in Third and Fourth Moods of First Figure
Author discusses why major/minor cannot be proven in third and fourth moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the major premise cannot be proven in the third mood and why neither premise can be proven in the fourth mood of the first figure using the described method.

Core Idea
Particular premises and negative conversions block proof paths in later moods.

The author applies his method to the third and fourth moods of the first figure, revealing where it succeeds and where it fails. This teaches the precise conditions under which the technique works, highlighting the role of quantity and quality in premises.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the third mood condition
In the third mood, both premises are particular (e.g., Some B is A, Some C is B). The conclusion is also particular.
The author states the major cannot be proven because both premises are particular, making conversion inapplicable.
2
Understand why the major fails in third mood
To prove the major, you would need to use the conclusion and conversion of the minor. But both are particular, so no conversion yields a universal. Therefore the method cannot be applied.
The minor conversion would also be particular, insufficient to connect with the conclusion.
3
See how the minor can still be proven in third mood
The minor can be proven by using the conclusion and conversion of the major (since the major is universal affirmative in the standard first figure moods? Actually in third mood the major is universal? Wait, the third mood has particular premises, but the author says the minor can be proven by the conclusion and conversion of the major. This suggests the major is universal affirmative in that mood? The text: 'As for the third mood... the major cannot be proven... the minor can be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the major.' So the major must be universal affirmative. That fits the standard first figure third mood: Some C is B, Every B is A? Actually first figure moods: (1) Barbara, (2) Celarent, (3) Darii, (4) Ferio. The third mood (Darii) has major universal affirmative and minor particular affirmative. So the minor can be proven as shown.
4
Analyze the fourth mood failure
In the fourth mood (Ferio), the major is universal negative, minor is particular affirmative. The conclusion is particular negative. The major cannot be proven because of the same reason as third mood (conclusion is particular). The minor cannot be proven because converting the conclusion gives a negative, and a negative is not suitable as a minor in the first figure unless it is a special negative as previously described.
Thus neither premise is provable by this method.

مسلوبا عن كل ما ليس موضوعا بالموضوع. ومثل هذه السالبة ما يرجع إلى العدول الموجب فينعكس بالإيجاب كما في قولنا لا شيء من الجوهر بعرض فإن هذا السلب مخصوص بالجوهر لما أن كل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض فينعكس إذن إلى قولنا كل ما ليس بعرض فهو جوهر وإلا فبعض ما ليس بعرض ليس بجوهر، وكل ما ليس بجوهر فهو عرض ينتج بعض ما ليس بعرض فهو عرض ؛ وإنه محال . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إذا كان المراد إثبات الموجبة في قولنا كل ج) ب) ، ولا شيء من ب أن كان من اللوازم أن تحوّل النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة لوجود موضوعهما ويقال كل (ج)) هو ليس «أ»، وكل ما ليس «أ» فهو «ب» لينتج كل (ج) ب»؛ فقولنا وكل ما ليس «أ» فهو «ب» من لوازم السالبة التي مرّ ذكرها. وأما الضرب الثالث منه فظاهر لما أن الكبرى منه لا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى لكونهما جزئيتين فـ . فأما الصغرى فيمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى كما يقال بعض (ج) أ»، وكل «أب ينتج بعض (ج) ب». وأما الضرب الرابع منه فلا يمكن بيان شيء من مقدمتيه بالنتيجة وعكس الأخرى؛ أما الكبرى فلما مرَّ في الضرب الثالث، وأما الصغرى فلأن عكس النتيجة سالبة والسالبة لا تصلح للصغروية في الشكل الأول إلا إذا كانت السالبة من جنس ما مر ذكــره. وحينئذ تحول النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضاف إليها ما يلزم السالبة من العكس حتى ينتج الصغرى بعينها.
Negated of everything that is not a subject by the subject. And an example of such a negative is what reverts to the positive with qualification, so it converts to the positive as in our saying: No substance is an accident. For this negation is specific to substance because everything that is not a substance is an accident, so it converts then to our saying: Everything that is not an accident is a substance. Otherwise, some of what is not an accident is not a substance, and everything that is not a substance is an accident, producing: Some of what is not an accident is an accident; and that is impossible. If you understand this, we say: If the intention is to prove the affirmative in our saying: Every J is B, and No B is A, then it is among the necessary consequences that the conclusion be transformed into the modified affirmative due to the existence of their subjects, and it is said: Every J is not A, and Everything that is not A is B, so that it produces: Every J is B. So our saying: Everything that is not A is B is among the necessary consequences of the negative that has been mentioned previously. As for the third mood of it, it is apparent because the major premise in it cannot be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the minor, because they are both particular. So as for the minor, it can be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the major, as is said: Some J is A, and Every A is B, producing: Some J is B. As for the fourth mood of it, neither of its two premises can be shown by the conclusion and the conversion of the other. As for the major, due to what passed in the third mood; as for the minor, because the conversion of the conclusion is negative, and the negative is not suitable for being the minor in the first figure unless the negative is of the type that was mentioned previously. And then the conclusion is transformed into the modified affirmative, and there is added to it what is necessary for the negative from conversion, until it produces the minor itself.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage explains how to prove the major premise of a syllogism in the first figure using conversion and the special nature of certain negative propositions. The author begins by discussing a type of negative proposition that is specific to a subject and can be converted into a modified affirmative (ma'dula). For example, the proposition 'No substance is an accident' is specific to substance because everything non-substance is an accident. This converts to 'Everything that is not an accident is a substance', but if the conversion were not so specific, it might lead to an impossibility. The author then applies this to proving the conclusion. When we want to prove the major premise 'Every J is B' and the minor is 'No B is A', we transform the conclusion into a modified affirmative: 'Every J is not A', and then use the necessary consequence of the negative: 'Everything that is not A is B', which together produce the desired conclusion. The author then discusses the third and fourth moods of the first figure. In the third mood (both premises particular), the major cannot be proven via conversion, but the minor can be proven by concluding 'Some J is B' and converting the major 'Every B is A'. In the fourth mood, neither premise can be shown directly due to the negative conversion of the conclusion being unsuitable for the minor, unless the negative is of the special type, in which case the conclusion is transformed into a modified affirmative and combined with the necessary consequence to produce the minor.

Key Terms
الضرب الثالث
الضرب الرابع
جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fourth mood of the first figure, why can the minor premise not be proven using the method?
A) Because the conclusion is negative and its conversion is also negative, which cannot be a minor in first figure unless it is a special negative.
B) Because the conclusion is particular affirmative.
C) Because the major is universal affirmative.
D) Because the minor is universal.

Key Takeaway
The method to prove premises by conclusion and conversion works only when the involved propositions are universal and affirmative, failing for particular or negative conversions outside special cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
For the fourth mood: 'No J is A; Some B is J; therefore Some B is not A.' Explain why the minor cannot be proven by the method unless the conclusion's conversion is a special negative.

Second Figure and the Refutation of Circular Proof
Author discusses proving premises in second figure and argues certain proofs are not circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proof in the second figure is genuinely circular and when it is not, based on the Shaykh's condition.

Core Idea
Not every proof using conversion from the conclusion is circular; the Shaykh's condition clarifies the distinction.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle point about syllogistic demonstration: when you prove a premise of a syllogism using the conclusion and conversion, does that make the proof circular? By analyzing the second figure in detail and invoking Ibn Sina's condition, he shows that circularity depends on whether the conversion is a genuine logical step or merely a necessary consequence. This lesson will walk you through his argument and help you distinguish circular from non-circular proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism in the first mood of the second figure.
Find the premises and conclusion in the author's example: 'Every J is B, No A is B, therefore No A is J'. This is a valid categorical syllogism in Cesare mood.
The terms J, B, A are placeholders for concrete Islamic examples.
2
Trace how the major premise is proven using the conclusion.
The author shows: take the conclusion 'No A is J', convert the minor to 'Every B is J', then form a new syllogism: 'Every B is J, No J is A, therefore No B is A', which converts to the original major 'No A is B'.
This demonstrates that the major can be proven without circularity.
3
Apply the Shaykh's condition for circular proof.
Ibn Sina said: if the only operation is taking the conclusion and converting one premise, then it is not circular. Here, the conversion of the minor is a necessary consequence, not a direct conversion from the conclusion.
The author argues that this proof meets the condition, so it is not circular.
4
Examine the case of the affirmative minor and the special negative type.
When the negative is of the special type, the negative conclusion can be transformed into a modified affirmative and combined with a necessary consequence to prove the minor, still avoiding circularity.
This handles the limitation that two negatives cannot produce a syllogism.

وأما الشكل الثاني فالمقدمة السالبة في الضرب الأول مما يمكن إثباته بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى الموجبة كما في قولنا كل ج) ب»، ولا شيء من «أب»، فإنه يمكن إثبات الكبرى بالعكس مع النتيجة فيقال كل ب ج ، ولا شيء من (ج) أ» فلا شيء من ب أ)؛ وإنه ينعكس إلى الكبرى السالبة وهي قولنا لا شيء من «أب». والشيخ قال في هذا المقام: «فإن كان من شرائط البيان الدوري أن تكون النتيجة تؤخذ ويعكس مقدمه فقط ولا عكس بعد ذلك لم يكن هذا البيان بيانًا دوريّا». والحق فيه أنه لا يكون بيانا دوريا؛ إذ البيان الدوري من جملة ما ينافي المتوسط بين المؤثر والأثر؛ وإنه مما يحققه. ولأن التعريف المشهور للدور يشعر بأن ذلك من الشرائط. ولو كان ذلك من الشرائط فلا يكون هذا البيان بيانًا دوريا. ولا يقال: إنه إذا لم يكن دوريا كان ذكره في باب الدور لا يليق بالحكم لما أنه يوهم أن يكون منه فإنه لا يكون موهما ؛ وقد أشار إلى شيء ينافي ذلك الوهم. وأما الصغرى الموجبة فلا يمكن إثباتها بالبيان الدوري أيضًا؛ إذ النتيجة سالبة، وعكس الكبرى السالبة أيضًا سالبة؛ ولا قياس من سالبتين. اللهم إلا أن تكون السالبة من الجنس الذي ذكرناه؛ وحينئذ تحوّل النتيجة السالبة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضم إلى لازم السالبة كما في قولنا كل «ج» ليس «أ»، وكل ما ليس «أ» فهو «ب» ينتج كل (ج) ب). ولقائل أن يقول إنه لا يكون بيانًا دوريًا لما أن عكس المقدمة من اللوازم ولا وجود للعكس فيه. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن المعنى من العكس في هذا الباب ما يجري مجرى العكس لا هو بعينه؛ غير أنه ضعيف. والحق فيه أنه لا يكون بيانًا دوريًا. وأما الضرب الثاني منه فذلك مثل قولنا لا شيء من ج ب ، وكل «أب» فالصغرى السالبة مما يمكن إثباته بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى كما في قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ»، وكل ب أ) فلا شيء من (ج) ب). وأما الكبرى الموجبة فلا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى لما مر إلا إذا كانت السالبة من الجنس الذي مرَّ ذكره؛ وحينئذ تحوّل عكس النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضاف إلى لازم السالبة لينتج الكبرى هكذا «أ» ليس (ج)، وكل ما ليس (ج) فهو (ب) ينتج كل (أب) وهو الكبرى؛ وهذا البيان
As for the second figure, the negative premise in the first mood is among what can be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the affirmative minor, as in our saying: Every J is B, and No A is B, for it is possible to prove the major by the conversion with the conclusion, so it is said: Every B is J, and No J is A, so No B is A; and it converts to the negative major which is our saying: No A is B. And the Shaykh said in this context: 'If it is among the conditions of circular proof that the conclusion is taken and only its premise is converted, and no further conversion, then this proof is not circular.' And the truth is that it is not a circular proof; for circular proof is among that which contradicts the intermediary between the effect and the cause; and it is something that he confirms. And because the well-known definition of circular proof indicates that this is among the conditions. And if that were among the conditions, then this proof would not be circular. And it cannot be said: If it were not circular, its mention in the chapter on circular proof would not be appropriate, because it might be thought to be from it, but it is not misleading; and he has indicated something that contradicts that misconception. As for the affirmative minor, it cannot be proven by circular proof also; for the conclusion is negative, and the conversion of the negative major is also negative; and there is no syllogism from two negatives. Unless the negative is of the type we mentioned; and then the negative conclusion is transformed into the modified affirmative and joined with the necessary consequence of the negative, as in our saying: Every J is not A, and Everything that is not A is B, producing Every J is B. And someone may say that it is not a circular proof because the conversion of the premise is from the necessary consequences and there is no actual conversion in it. And it can be answered that the meaning of conversion in this chapter is that which runs in the course of conversion, not the same thing; but that is weak. And the truth is that it is not a circular proof. As for the second mood of it, that is like our saying: No J is B, and Every A is B, so the negative minor is among what can be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the major, as in our saying: No J is A, and Every A is not B? Wait: The text says: 'لا شيء من ج ب، وكل أب' so minor: No J is B, major: Every A is B. Then he says: 'فالصغرى السالبة مما يمكن إثباته بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى كما في قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ، وكل ب أ) فلا شيء من (ج) ب.' That translates: 'so the negative minor is among what can be proven by the conclusion and the conversion of the major, as in our saying: No J is A, and Every B is A, so No J is B.' And then: 'وأما الكبرى الموجبة فلا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى لما مر إلا إذا كانت السالبة من الجنس الذي مرَّ ذكره؛ وحينئذ تحوّل عكس النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضاف إلى لازم السالبة لينتج الكبرى هكذا «أ» ليس (ج)، وكل ما ليس (ج) فهو (ب) ينتج كل (أب) وهو الكبرى؛ وهذا البيان'
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage deals with the second figure of the categorical syllogism and the concept of circular proof (burhan dauri). The author first explains how in the first mood of the second figure, the negative major premise can be proven by using the conclusion and converting the minor. For example, given 'Every J is B' and 'No A is B', we can say 'Every B is J' (conversion of the minor) and combine it with 'No J is A' (the conclusion considered as a premise) to get 'No B is A', which is the conversion of the original major. The author then mentions the statement of the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina) that if the condition for a circular proof is that only one premise is converted from the conclusion, then this is not circular. The author affirms this, stating that circular proof would violate the intermediary between cause and effect. He then addresses the affirmative minor: it cannot be proven via circular proof because the conclusion is negative and the conversion of the major is also negative, and two negatives do not yield a syllogism. However, if the negative is of the special type discussed earlier, the negative conclusion can be transformed into a modified affirmative and combined with the necessary consequence of the negative to prove the minor. A potential objector might argue that this is not circular because the conversion is actually a necessary consequence, not a direct conversion. The author notes a weak response that 'conversion' here means something that runs in its course, but he asserts the truth that it is not circular. Then he discusses the second mood: 'No J is B, Every A is B'. Here the negative minor can be proven by the conclusion and conversion of the major: using 'No J is A' (conclusion) and 'Every A is B'? Wait, the text gives: 'لا شيء من (ج) أ، وكل ب أ) فلا شيء من (ج) ب.' That is: 'No J is A, and Every B is A, so No J is B.' So the minor 'No J is B' is proven. The affirmative major cannot be proven unless the negative is of the special type, then the conversion of the conclusion is transformed into a modified affirmative and combined with the necessary consequence to produce 'Every A is B'.

Key Terms
بيان دوري (bayan dauri): Circular proof, where a premise is proven using the conclusion without a genuine independent middle term; the author argues certain proofs avoid this. [Note: In later dialectical works, circular proof is considered a fallacy; here it is discussed in relation to the conditions of demonstration.]
عكس المقدمة ('aks al-muqaddima): Conversion of a premise, i.e., swapping subject and predicate while preserving truth; in this context, it may be a necessary consequence rather than literal conversion.
لازم السالبة (lazim al-saliba): A necessary consequence that follows from the negative proposition when it is of the special type, used to construct a modified affirmative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the conclusion and the conversion of the major are both negative
C) Because the minor is universal
D) Because the major is affirmative

Key Takeaway
A proof in the second figure that uses conversion from the conclusion is not circular if the conversion is a necessary consequence and the conditions for circularity are not met.

First Type: Positive Minor Premise in Circular Demonstration
Author explains why a positive minor premise cannot be proven circularly unless special treatment is applied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a positive minor premise cannot be proven by circular demonstration and evaluate the proposed exception using the rule of two negatives.

Core Idea
A positive minor premise cannot be proven circularly because it would require two negative premises.

In this lesson, we examine the first type of circular demonstration, focusing on proving a positive minor premise. The author argues that it is impossible under normal conditions because the conclusion and the converse of the major are both negative, and two negatives cannot form a valid syllogism. We then explore a special case where the negative is transformed into a positive modified proposition, and see why this still does not constitute true circular demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about positive minor premise
The author states that a positive minor premise cannot be proven by circular demonstration. Locate this claim in the text and understand it as the starting point.
2
Understand the logical obstacle
The reason given is that the conclusion (of the circular demonstration) is negative, and the converse of the negative major premise is also negative. Since no syllogism can be formed from two negative premises, the attempt fails.
Recall the rule: two negative premises yield no necessary conclusion.
3
Examine the exception
The author mentions a special type of negative that can be converted into a positive modified proposition (al-mawjuba al-ma'dula), combined with the necessary concomitant of the negative. Study the example: 'Every J is not A; everything that is not A is B; therefore every J is B.'
This yields a positive minor, but is it truly circular?
4
Evaluate the objection and the author's verdict
An objection is raised that this is not a circular demonstration because the 'converse' used is actually a necessary concomitant, not a proper converse. The author mentions a weak answer and then concludes that it is indeed not a circular demonstration.
Consider why the author's final position aligns with the original condition.

وأما الصغرى الموجبة فلا يمكن إثباتها بالبيان الدوري أيضًا؛ إذ النتيجة سالبة، وعكس الكبرى السالبة أيضًا سالبة؛ ولا قياس من سالبتين. اللهم إلا أن تكون السالبة من الجنس الذي ذكرناه؛ وحينئذ تحوّل النتيجة السالبة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضم إلى لازم السالبة كما في قولنا كل «ج» ليس «أ»، وكل ما ليس «أ» فهو «ب» ينتج كل (ج) ب). ولقائل أن يقول إنه لا يكون بيانًا دوريًا لما أن عكس المقدمة من اللوازم ولا وجود للعكس فيه. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن المعنى من العكس في هذا الباب ما يجري مجرى العكس لا هو بعينه؛ غير أنه ضعيف. والحق فيه أنه لا يكون بيانًا دوريًا.
As for the positive minor premise, it cannot be proven by circular demonstration either, because the conclusion is negative, and the converse of the negative major is also negative; and there is no syllogism from two negatives. Unless the negative is of the type we mentioned; then the negative conclusion is converted into a positive modified [proposition] and is appended to the necessary [concomitant] of the negative, as in our statement: 'Every J is not A, and everything that is not A is B' produces 'Every J is B.' And it may be said that it is not a circular demonstration because the converse of the premise is among the necessary concomitants and there is no converse in it. And it is possible to answer that the meaning of 'converse' in this chapter is what runs in the course of the converse, not the converse itself; but this is weak. And the truth is that it is not a circular demonstration.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage discusses the first type of circular demonstration, focusing on proving a positive minor premise. The author states that a positive minor cannot be proven circularly because the conclusion is negative and the converse of the major negative premise is also negative, and no syllogism can be formed from two negative premises. However, if the negative is of a special type previously mentioned (possibly a negative with a necessary concomitant), then the negative conclusion can be transformed into a positive modified proposition (al-mawjuba al-ma'dula) and combined with the necessary concomitant of the negative. For example: 'Every J is not A; everything that is not A is B; therefore every J is B.' This yields the positive minor. But then a questioner objects that this is not a true circular demonstration because the converse of the premise is actually a necessary concomitant, not a proper converse. The author acknowledges this objection and mentions a weak answer that 'converse' here is used loosely. Ultimately, the author concludes that this is still not a circular demonstration, consistent with the earlier condition.

Second Type of Circular Demonstration: Proving the Negative Minor
Author explains the second type of circular demonstration for proving negative minor and positive major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premise in a categorical syllogism can be proven cyclically according to the second type, and reconstruct the proof steps.

Core Idea
In circular demonstration, the negative minor is provable, but the positive major is not—unless the negative is of a rare type.

In the previous lesson, we examined the first type of circular demonstration. Now the author turns to the second type, where the minor premise is negative and the major is positive. He provides an example: 'No J is B, and every A is B.' He then shows that the negative minor (no J is B) can be proven using the conclusion and the converse of the major. However, the positive major (every A is B) cannot be proven in the usual way, unless the negative premise is of a special type that allows the transformation of the converse of the conclusion into a positive modified proposition. This lesson will guide you through these logical moves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises of the original syllogism
The author gives the example: 'No J is B' (the negative minor) and 'Every A is B' (the positive major). Note that J, B, A are placeholders for terms. Write down the syllogism in standard form, optionally filling with Islamic terms such as J = khamr (wine), B = halal (permissible), A = nabidh (date wine).
For instance: No khamr is halal; every nabidh is khamr.
2
Determine the conclusion of the original syllogism
From 'No J is B' and 'Every A is B', using a valid categorical syllogism (second figure, Cesare), the conclusion is 'No J is A'.
Check with a Venn diagram or rules of syllogism: E-A-E in second figure is valid.
3
Prove the negative minor cyclically
Take the conclusion 'No J is A' and the converse of the major. The author uses 'Every B is A' (a stronger converse). With 'No J is A' and 'Every B is A', we deduce 'No J is B', which is the original minor. This proves the minor cyclically.
The author's example uses 'Every B is A' rather than the partial converse 'Some B is A', indicating a special condition.
4
Attempt to prove the positive major cyclically and understand the exception
Using the conclusion and the converse of the minor ('No B is J') fails to produce the major. The author notes that only if the negative is of a special type can the converse of the conclusion be transformed into a positive modified proposition, which with the necessary concomitant yields the major. But even then it is not truly circular.
This exception is rare and does not satisfy the strict definition of circular demonstration.

وأما الضرب الثاني منه فذلك مثل قولنا لا شيء من ج ب ، وكل «أب» فالصغرى السالبة مما يمكن إثباته بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى كما في قولنا لا شيء من (ج) أ»، وكل ب أ) فلا شيء من (ج) ب). وأما الكبرى الموجبة فلا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى لما مر إلا إذا كانت السالبة من الجنس الذي مرَّ ذكره؛ وحينئذ تحوّل عكس النتيجة إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضاف إلى لازم السالبة لينتج الكبرى هكذا «أ» ليس (ج)، وكل ما ليس (ج) فهو (ب) ينتج كل (أب) وهو الكبرى؛ وهذا البيان أيضًا في حيز المنع فإنه لا يكون بيانا دوريا إذا كان الدور عبارة عما قالوه.
As for the second type of it, that is like our statement: 'No J is B, and every A is B.' So the negative minor premise is among what can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, as in our statement: 'No J is A, and every B is A' gives 'No J is B.' As for the positive major, it cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the minor for the reason mentioned, unless the negative is of the type previously mentioned; then the converse of the conclusion is converted into the positive modified and is added to the necessary concomitant of the negative to produce the major, thus: 'A is not J, and everything that is not J is B' produces 'Every A is B,' which is the major. And this demonstration is also subject to prevention, for it is not a circular demonstration if the circle is defined as they said.

Commentary
This passage presents the second type of circular demonstration. The author gives an example: 'No J is B, and every A is B.' Here the negative minor premise can be proven cyclically by using the conclusion and the converse of the major: from 'No J is A' and 'Every B is A' we deduce 'No J is B.' However, the positive major premise (every A is B) cannot be proven cyclically by the usual method. Unless the negative is of a special type, in which case the converse of the conclusion is transformed into a positive modified proposition and combined with the necessary concomitant of the negative to produce the major: 'A is not J' and 'Everything not J is B' yields 'Every A is B.' But the author notes that even this demonstration is not truly circular according to the definition of circle previously given, so it is subject to the same objection.

Key Terms

Proving the Minor Premise via Transformation
Author explains the third type where neither premise can be proven normally, but the minor can be proven via special transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the special transformation method to prove a particular minor premise from a particular negative conclusion and a universal affirmative premise.

Core Idea
A particular negative conclusion can be transformed into a positive to prove a particular minor.

This passage introduces a special case in syllogistic reasoning where neither premise can be proven by standard cyclical methods due to logical constraints (two particulars or two negatives). However, by converting a particular negative conclusion into a positive modified proposition and combining it with a universal premise about the necessary concomitant of the negative, we can derive the original particular minor premise. This method mirrors the first type but applies to particular propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of negative
Recognize that the negative proposition must be of the specific type that allows conversion to a positive modified, as mentioned in the first type.
2
Convert the negative conclusion
Transform 'Some J is not A' into a positive modified proposition, e.g., 'Some J is non-A' (or 'some J is not-A') as an affirmative statement.
This conversion is only valid for the special type of negative.
3
Add the necessary concomitant
Take the universal proposition 'Everything that is not A is B' without any conversion of the negative.
This is the necessary concomitant of the negative term.
4
Form the syllogism
Combine the converted premise and the universal premise: 'Some J is non-A; every non-A is B; therefore some J is B.'
This is a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure (Darii).
5
Recognize the result
The conclusion 'Some J is B' is exactly the minor premise that needed to be proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the third type, its major cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the minor, because there is no syllogism from two particulars. As for its minor, likewise, because there is no syllogism from two negatives. But it can be proven by the method mentioned in the first type if the negative is of the type previously mentioned, namely by converting the negative conclusion into a positive modified and appending it to the necessary concomitant of the negative without conversion, thus: 'Some J is not A, and everything that is not A is B' produces 'Some J is B,' which is the minor itself.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage addresses the third type. The author states that the major premise cannot be proven cyclically because the conclusion and the converse of the minor are both particular, and no syllogism results from two particular propositions. The minor also cannot be proven directly because it would require two negatives. However, if the negative is of the special type described earlier, the minor can be proven. This is done by converting the negative conclusion (which is 'some J is not A') into a positive modified proposition and then combining it with the necessary concomitant of the negative without any conversion. For example: 'Some J is not A; everything that is not A is B; therefore some J is B.' This yields the original minor premise. The method mirrors that of the first type but is applied to particular propositions.

Fourth Type: Proving the Minor, Not the Major
Author explains the fourth type of circular syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a particular negative premise in a circular syllogism can be proven, and identify when convertibility blocks circular proof.

Core Idea
Particular negatives never convert; this single rule blocks circular proof of the major in the fourth type.

In circular demonstrations (burhan dawri), we try to prove a premise from the conclusion. The fourth type has a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. The major cannot be proven, but the minor can. Let's see why and how.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fourth type's structure
In the fourth type, the minor premise is a particular negative (e.g., Some J is not B), the major is universal affirmative (Every B is A), and the conclusion is Some J is not A. Recognize this pattern.
The letters A, B, J are placeholders for terms.
2
Check convertibility of the minor
The minor is a particular negative proposition. In logic, particular negatives (some S is not P) are not convertible. That means you cannot deduce 'some B is not J' from 'some J is not B'.
Contrast with universal negative (no S is P) which does convert to 'no P is S'.
3
Attempt to prove the major
To prove the major (Every B is A), you would need to use the conclusion (Some J is not A) and the converse of the minor. Since the minor does not convert, that path fails. Even if it were convertible, the premises would become two particular negatives (Some J is not A and Some B is not J), which form no valid syllogism.
Two particular premises never yield a valid categorical syllogism in any figure.
4
Prove the minor successfully
To prove the minor (Some J is not B), use the conclusion (Some J is not A) and the major premise (Every B is A). From 'Some J is not A' and 'Every B is A' we deduce 'Some J is not B' – a valid second‑figure syllogism (mood: Felapton? Actually, it's Camestres? Let's check: The premises are: Some J is not A (O) and Every B is A (A). Conclusion: Some J is not B (O). This is valid in the second figure (AOO? No, second figure requires one negative premise. Actually, it's the mood 'Festino'? Festino is EIO in second figure. Here we have O and A giving O. That is actually a valid mood sometimes called 'Bocardo' in third figure? Wait, let's not get bogged down. The author presents it as straightforward.)
The author describes this proof as using 'the converse of the major' but the quoted premises use the major itself. This may be a textual nuance; the key is that the proof succeeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الضرب الرابع فظاهر أيضًا أن كبراه لا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى؛ إذ الصغرى غير منعكسة، ولو كانت منعكسة فلا يمكن أيضًا؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين جزئيتين. وأما الصغرى فإنه يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى هكذا بعض «ج» ليس «أ»، وكل ب أ) فبعض «ج» ليس (ب) وهو المطلوب.
As for the fourth type, it is also apparent that its major cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the minor, because the minor is not convertible; and even if it were convertible, it would also not be possible, because there is no syllogism from two particular negatives. As for the minor, it can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, thus: 'Some J is not A, and every B is A' gives 'Some J is not B,' which is what is sought.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth type. The author states that the major premise cannot be proven cyclically because the minor premise is not convertible. Even if it were convertible, the result would still not work because it would involve two particular negatives, which do not yield a valid syllogism. However, the minor premise can be proven. The proof uses the conclusion and the converse of the major: from 'Some J is not A' and 'Every B is A' we deduce 'Some J is not B.' This conclusion is exactly the minor premise (since the minor is 'Some J is not B'). So this type of circular demonstration works for the minor but not the major.

Proving Premises in the Third Figure
Author explains that in the third figure, the universal premise cannot be proven, but particular ones may be proven under certain conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given premise in a third-figure categorical syllogism can be proven by circular demonstration, and identify the conditions under which such proof is possible.

Core Idea
In the third figure, only certain particular premises can be proven by circular demonstration; universal premises are unprovable.

In circular demonstration (burhān dawrī), we use the conclusion of a syllogism together with the converse of a premise to prove that premise. In the third figure, the conclusion is always particular. This imposes strict limits on which premises can be proven. This lesson unpacks those limits, showing you exactly when a premise in the third figure can and cannot be proven.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the premise to be proven
First, note whether the premise you want to prove is universal or particular. If it is universal, the author says it cannot be proven at all because the conclusion is particular and combining it with any converse yields only a particular.
Universal premise = muqaddima kulliyya.
2
2. If particular, determine its position
Is the particular premise the major (kubrā) or the minor (sughrā)? The author treats each case separately. This affects which conversions and which premise qualities are allowed.
3
3. Check the quality of the particular premise
For a particular major: if it is positive, it cannot be proven because the conclusion is positive and together with the converse of the minor it does not yield a valid syllogism in the third figure. If it is negative, it can be proven by conjoining the converse of the minor with the conclusion.
The example: 'Every J is B; some J is not A → some B is not A' proves the major 'some B is not A'.
4
4. If the particular is minor, check the major
If the major is universal positive, the minor can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major. If the major is negative, the minor cannot be proven because that would require two negatives, which is invalid.
Example: 'Some J is A; every A is B → some J is B' proves the minor 'some J is A' after conversion of the conclusion.
5
5. Summarize the conditions
Universal premise: never provable. Particular major negative: provable. Particular minor with universal positive major: provable. All other combinations: not provable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثالث / فالمقدمة الكلية لا يمكن إثباتها البتة؛ إذ النتيجة الجزئية مع عكس مقدمة كيف كانت لا تنتج إلا جزئية. وأما الجزئية فإن كانت كبرى فإما أن تكون موجبة ; وحينئذ تكون النتيجة موجبة لا محالة، وإنها مع عكس الصغرى لا تنتج ; إذ لا قياس عن صغرى موجبة كلية وكبرى موجبة جزئية في هذا الشكل وهو ينتج الكبرى وإما أن تكون سالبة; وحينئذ يمكن إثباتها بعكس الصغرى مع النتيجة السالبة من هذا الشكل هكذا كل «ج ب»، وبعض «ج» ليس «أ» ينتج بعض «ب» ليس «أ» . وإن كانت الجزئية صغرى فالكبرى إما أن تكون موجبة كلية; وحينئذ يمكن إثبات الصغرى بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى هكذا بعض «ج أ»، وكل «أب» ينتج بعض «ج ب» وإنه ينعكس إلى الصغرى. وإما أن تكون سالبة وإنها أي الصغرى من جملة ما لا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى حينئذ ; إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين. اللهم
As for the third figure, the universal premise cannot be proven at all, because the particular conclusion together with the converse of any premise produces only a particular. As for the particular, if it is major, then either it is positive; in which case the conclusion is necessarily positive, and it together with the converse of the minor does not produce [the major], because there is no syllogism from a universal positive minor and a particular positive major in this figure; and it produces the major. Or it is negative; in which case it can be proven by the converse of the minor with the negative conclusion from this figure, thus: 'Every J is B, and some J is not A' produces 'Some B is not A.' And if the particular is minor, then the major is either a universal positive; in which case the minor can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, thus: 'Some J is A, and every A is B' produces 'Some J is B,' which converts to the minor. Or it is negative, and the minor is among what cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major at that time, because there is no syllogism from two negatives. O Allah.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage addresses circular demonstration in the third figure. The author begins by stating that a universal premise in the third figure cannot be proven at all because the conclusion is particular, and combining a particular conclusion with the converse of any premise only yields a particular result, never a universal. Then he discusses particular premises. If the particular is the major premise, there are two cases: if it is positive, the conclusion is positive (since in the third figure with positive premises the conclusion is always particular positive), but combining it with the converse of the minor does not produce the major because that would require a syllogism with a universal positive minor and a particular positive major, which is invalid in this figure. If the particular major is negative, it can be proven by taking the converse of the minor (which is universal positive) together with the negative conclusion, e.g., 'Every J is B; some J is not A; therefore some B is not A.' If the particular is the minor, and the major is universal positive, the minor can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, e.g., 'Some J is A; every A is B; therefore some J is B,' and then converting 'some J is B' to 'some B is J' gives the minor (some B is J?). Actually the minor here is 'some J is A'? The example shows 'some J is A' as the minor, so the conversion works. If the major is negative, the minor cannot be proven due to two negatives. The passage ends with 'اللهم' (O Allah), possibly a prayer or a placeholder.

Circular Proof in the Third Figure: Which Premises Can Be Proven?
Author determines which premises in the third figure can be proven circularly, with special exception for certain negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which premises in a third-figure categorical syllogism can be proven by circular demonstration, and identify the exceptional case.

Core Idea
The third figure's particular conclusion restricts circular proof to only certain particular premises.

Circular demonstration aims to prove a premise using the conclusion and the other premise. But in the third figure, the conclusion is always particular. This lesson systematically examines which premises can be proven circularly, following the author's detailed case analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion (some S is P or some S is not P). This is the starting constraint.
A universal premise cannot be proven because the conclusion is particular.
2
Test the universal premise
If we try to prove a universal premise (major or minor), the particular conclusion plus a converse gives only a particular. Therefore, no universal premise can be proven circularly in the third figure.
3
Examine the particular major affirmative
If the major is particular affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative. Attempting circular proof using the converse of the minor fails because the third figure requires a universal major for validity – here we have a universal minor and particular major, which is an invalid mood.
Thus the particular major affirmative cannot be proven.
4
Examine the particular major negative
If the major is particular negative, it can be proven. Use the converse of the minor (universal affirmative) and the negative conclusion: from 'Every J is B' and 'Some J is not A' we get 'Some B is not A', which is the major.
5
Examine the particular minor with universal affirmative major
If the minor is particular and the major is universal affirmative, the minor can be proven. Use the conclusion and the converse of the major: from 'Some J is A' and 'Every A is B' we get 'Some J is B', which converts to 'Some B is J' – that is the minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثالث فالمقدمة الكلية لا يمكن إثباتها البتة؛ إذ النتيجة الجزئية مع عكس مقدمة كيف كانت لا تنتج إلا جزئية. وأما الجزئية فإن كانت كبرى فإما أن تكون موجبة؛ وحينئذ تكون النتيجة موجبة لا محالة، وإنها مع عكس الصغرى لا تنتج؛ إذ لا قياس عن صغرى موجبة كلية وكبرى موجبة جزئية في هذا الشكل وهو ينتج الكبرى وإما أن تكون سالبة؛ وحينئذ يمكن إثباتها بعكس الصغرى مع النتيجة السالبة من هذا الشكل هكذا كل «ج ب»، وبعض «ج» ليس «أ» ينتج بعض «ب» ليس «أ». وإن كانت الجزئية صغرى فالكبرى إما أن تكون موجبة كلية؛ وحينئذ يمكن إثبات الصغرى بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى هكذا بعض «ج أ»، وكل «أب» ينتج بعض «ج ب» وإنه ينعكس إلى الصغرى. وإما أن تكون سالبة وإنها أي الصغرى من جملة ما لا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى حينئذ؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين. اللهم إلا أن تكون السالبة الكلية من الجنس الذي مرَّ ذكره فإنه يمكن على النحو الذي ذكرناه فيقال بعض «ج» ليس «أ»، وكل ما ليس (أ) فهو «ب» ينتج بعض ج ب»؛ وإنه ينعكس إلى الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية.
And as for the third figure, the universal premise cannot be proven at all, because the particular conclusion, together with the converse of any premise, only produces a particular. As for the particular, if it is the major, then either it is affirmative; in that case the conclusion is necessarily affirmative, and it together with the converse of the minor does not produce anything, because there is no syllogism from an affirmative universal minor and an affirmative particular major in this figure—and it would produce the major—or it is negative; in that case it can be proven by the converse of the minor together with the negative conclusion from this figure, like this: Every J is B, and some J is not A, yields Some B is not A. And if the particular is the minor, then the major is either affirmative universal; in that case the minor can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, like this: Some J is A, and every A is B, yields Some J is B, and it converts to the minor. Or it is negative, and in that case the minor is among those that cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major, because there is no syllogism from two negatives. Unless the universal negative is of the type previously mentioned, for then it can be done in the way we mentioned, by saying: Some J is not A, and everything not A is B, yields Some J is B, and it converts to the affirmative particular minor.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage discusses circular demonstration in the third figure of the categorical syllogism. The third figure has a particular conclusion, so a universal premise cannot be proven circularly because it would require a universal result from particular premises, which is impossible. For particular premises, the author distinguishes cases. If the particular is the major premise and is affirmative, the conclusion is affirmative; but combining it with the converse of the minor yields no syllogism because the third figure requires a particular minor and universal major for a valid inference—here we have universal minor and particular major, which is invalid. If the particular major is negative, it can be proven by taking the converse of the minor (which is universal affirmative) and the negative conclusion: from 'Every J is B' and 'Some J is not A' we get 'Some B is not A', which is the major. If the particular is the minor, with a universal affirmative major, we can prove the minor by the conclusion and converse of the major: from 'Some J is A' and 'Every A is B' we get 'Some J is B', which converts to 'Some B is J', i.e., the minor. If the major is negative, the minor cannot be proven because two negatives yield no conclusion. However, there is an exception: if the universal negative is of the special type (allowing conversion to positive modified), then using the method of converting the conclusion to positive modified and using the necessary consequent, we can produce a syllogism resulting in the minor premise. The author illustrates: 'Some J is not A, and everything not A is B, yields Some J is B', which converts to the affirmative particular minor. This shows the flexibility of circular demonstration under certain conditions.
