Circular Demonstration in the Fourth Figure: Modes 1–3
Author tests circular proof in fourth figure modes 1–3.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which premises in the first three modes of the fourth figure can be proven by circular demonstration and explain why.

Core Idea
Circular demonstration in the fourth figure is highly restrictive: only one premise can be proven in mode 3.

The fourth figure presents unique challenges for circular demonstration. In this lesson, we analyze the first three modes to see which premises can be derived from the conclusion and a conversed premise. The author shows that only the particular affirmative major of the second mode and the universal negative minor of the third mode can be proven circularly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modes
The author discusses four modes of the fourth figure. Modes 1 and 2 are treated together, then mode 3 separately. Locate these in the text.
Mode 4 and 5 are discussed later.
2
Examine mode 1 and 2
For modes 1 and 2, the universal affirmative major cannot be proven. The author explains that the particular conclusion with a converse yields only a particular or nothing. Understand why: the major premise is universal affirmative, but the resources don't produce it.
3
Examine the particular affirmative in mode 2
The particular affirmative major of the second mode can be proven using the conclusion and the converse of the minor from the third figure. This is a special case.
The minor is from the third figure, showing cross-figure circularity.
4
Examine mode 3
The universal negative minor can be proven from the conclusion and the converse of the major from the second figure. The author gives an example: 'Every B is A, and no J is A, yields nothing B is J.'
The universal affirmative major cannot be proven because that would require two negatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الرابع فالموجبة الكلية من الضرب الأول والثاني منه لا يمكن إثباتها بالبيان الدوري؛ إذ النتيجة الجزئية مع عكس المقدمة الكلية لا تنتج إلا الجزئية، ومع عكس الجزئية لا تنتج شيئًا. وأما الموجبة الجزئية التي هي كبرى الضرب الثاني فإنه يمكن بيانها بالنتيجة مع عكس الصغرى من الثالث فإنه ينتج موجبة جزئية. وأما الضرب الثالث فالصغرى السالبة الكلية يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الكبرى من الشكل الثاني هكذا كل «ب أ»، ولا شيء من (ج) أ ينتج لا شيء من (ب) ج. وأما الكبرى الموجبة الكلية فلا يمكن إثباتها بالنتيجة وعكس الصغرى السالبة؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين. فأما إذا كانت السالبة التي هي الصغرى من الجنس الذي مر ذكره فحينئذ يمكن بأن تحوّل عكسها إلى الموجبة المعدولة ويضم إلى لازم السالبة من غير عكس فإنه ينتظم منهما قياس من الشكل الأول ينتج الكبرى الموجبة لكن من اللوازم أن يكون لعكس النتيجة موضوع موجود. وأما الضرب الرابع والخامس فلا يمكن بيان شيء من مقدمتيهما بالبيان الدوري لما أن نتيجة كل واحد منهما سالبة جزئية؛ وإنها مع عكس المقدمة موجبة كانت المقدمة أو سالبة لا ينتظم. فأما مع عكس الموجبة الكلية فإنه ينتج من الشكل الثالث بعض «ب» ليس «أ» وهو المطلوب. وقد ظهر بما ذكرناه أن البيان الدوري في الشكل الأول لا يخرج منه، وفي الشكل الثاني وغيره يخرج؛ لأنه في الثاني قد يكون منه، وقد يكون من الأول، وفي الثالث قد يكون من الأول، وفي الرابع قد يكون من الثاني، وقد يكون من الثالث كما مر.
As for the fourth figure, the universal affirmative from its first and second modes cannot be proven by circular demonstration, because the particular conclusion together with the converse of the universal premise only yields a particular, and together with the converse of the particular premise it yields nothing. As for the particular affirmative which is the major of the second mode, it can be proven by the conclusion together with the converse of the minor from the third figure, for it yields a particular affirmative. As for the third mode, the universal negative minor can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major from the second figure, like this: Every B is A, and no (J) is A, yields nothing B is J. As for the universal affirmative major, it cannot be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the negative minor, because there is no syllogism from two negatives. But if the negative, which is the minor, is of the type previously mentioned, then it can be done by converting its converse into a positive modified proposition and joining it to the necessary consequent of the negative without conversion, for then a syllogism from the first figure is formed that yields the affirmative major, but it is necessary that the converse of the conclusion have an existing subject. As for the fourth and fifth modes, nothing of their premises can be proven by circular demonstration, because the conclusion of each is a particular negative, and together with the converse of any premise, whether affirmative or negative, it does not yield a valid syllogism. But with the converse of the universal affirmative, it yields from the third figure 'Some B is not A', which is what is sought. And it has become clear from what we have said that circular demonstration does not leave the first figure; in the second and others it leaves, because in the second it may be from itself, and may be from the first; in the third it may be from the first; in the fourth it may be from the second, and may be from the third, as has passed.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage covers circular demonstration in the fourth figure, then concludes with a summary for all figures. In the first and second modes of the fourth figure, the universal affirmative major cannot be proven circularly because the particular conclusion only yields a particular when combined with the converse of any premise. However, the particular affirmative major of the second mode can be proven using the conclusion and the converse of the minor from the third figure, producing a particular affirmative. For the third mode, the universal negative minor can be proven by the conclusion and the converse of the major from the second figure: 'Every B is A' and 'No J is A' gives 'No B is J', which is the minor. The universal affirmative major cannot be proven due to two negatives. But if the negative minor is of the special type, one can convert its converse to a positive modified proposition and combine with the necessary consequent (without conversion) to form a first-figure syllogism that yields the major, provided the converse of the conclusion has an actual subject. The fourth and fifth modes have particular negative conclusions; no premise can be proven circularly because the conclusion with any converse fails to produce a valid syllogism, except that with the converse of a universal affirmative, it yields a particular negative from the third figure, which is the desired conclusion. The final paragraph summarizes that circular demonstration never leaves the first figure—meaning it is always ultimately reducible to the first figure. In the second figure, circular proof may come from itself (i.e., within the second figure) or from the first; in the third, from the first; in the fourth, from the second or third. This demonstrates the foundational role of the first figure in syllogistic logic.

البيان الدوري

C) Because the fourth figure is invalid.
D) Because the converse of the major is not available.

Key Takeaway
In the fourth figure, only the particular affirmative major of mode 2 and the universal negative minor of mode 3 can be proven by circular demonstration.

Circular Demonstration Across All Figures
Author summarizes which figures allow circular demonstration and their dependency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine from which figure a circular demonstration in a given figure must be drawn.

Core Idea
The first figure is the ultimate foundation for all circular demonstrations.

The author concludes his discussion of circular demonstration by summarizing how it operates across the four figures. The key insight is that circular demonstration never leaves the first figure—every successful circular proof ultimately relies on the first figure. In this lesson, we will map these dependencies.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The author says circular demonstration 'لا يخرج منه' (does not leave) the first figure. This means any circular proof can be reduced to the first figure.
2
Analyze the second figure
In the second figure, circular demonstration may come from itself (i.e., within the second figure) or from the first figure. Identify why it can come from itself.
This implies that some second-figure syllogisms can be proven circularly without leaving the figure.
3
Analyze the third figure
In the third figure, circular demonstration only comes from the first figure. So all circular proofs in the third figure are ultimately first-figure syllogisms.
4
Analyze the fourth figure
In the fourth figure, circular demonstration comes from either the second or the third figure. But since those eventually go to the first, the fourth figure's circular proofs also trace back to the first.

The Syllogism

True Premises Entail True Conclusion, But Not Conversely
Author states the principle that true premises necessitate a true conclusion, but the converse does not hold.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the truth of premises and the truth of conclusion in a syllogism, and identify cases where false premises yield a true conclusion, using examples from classical Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Truth of premises guarantees truth of conclusion, but a true conclusion does not guarantee true premises.

In this passage, the author explains a fundamental principle of logic: a valid syllogism with true premises must yield a true conclusion, but the converse does not hold. This means that a true conclusion can arise from false premises without invalidating the syllogism's form. Understanding this asymmetry is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences, where the soundness of a proof depends on both form and material truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The author states that true premises necessarily produce a true conclusion. This is the forward direction of entailment.
2
Understand the reversal denial
The author explicitly denies the reverse: a true conclusion does not imply true premises. This is the main epistemological point.
3
Examine the syllogism as antecedent-consequent
The syllogism (with true premises and correct form) is treated as an antecedent; the conclusion is the consequent. Removing the antecedent does not remove the consequent, but positing the antecedent posits the consequent.
This parallels conditional logic: if P then Q, not-Q implies not-P, but Q does not imply P.
4
Analyze the examples
The text gives two examples: one using terms ج, ب, أ in the second figure, and another using 'human', 'stone', 'animal'. In both, false premises still yield a true conclusion, demonstrating the principle.
Example 1: 'No ج is ب' and 'Every أ is ب' (false) produce 'No ج is أ' (true). Example 2: 'Every human is a stone' and 'Every stone is an animal' (false) produce 'Every human is an animal' (true).
5
Generalize the principle
Conclude that validity concerns form, while truth of premises is separate. A valid syllogism can have false premises and a true conclusion. This is why we must verify premises independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن المقدمات الصادقة تلزمها النتيجة الصادقة؛ ولا ينعكس فإن القياس نحو المقدم للنتيجة والنتيجة كالتالي ولا يلزم من رفع المقدم وهو القياس المشتمل على صدق المقدمات وصواب التأليف رفع التالي وهو النتيجة الصادقة لكن يلزم من وضعه وضع التالي على ما عرف. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فلا يمنع أن تلزم من المقدمات الكاذبة نتيجة صادقة لا لأن صدقها أو حقيقة تلك المقدّمات بل لأنه اتفق أن كانت صادقة لذاتها لكن انتظم على لزومها القياس المركَّب من تلك المقدمات الكاذبة. ومثال ذلك أنك إذا قلت كل ج ب ، ولا شيء من «أب» على أنهما صادقتان ينتج من الشكل الثاني لا شيء من (ج) أ»؛ ثم إذا جعلت الموجبة منهما سالبة والسالبة موجبة من غير تغير الحدود، وقلت لا شيء من (ج) ب ، وكل «أب» كانت كل واحدة منهما كاذبة حينئذ. ومع ذلك فالمؤلف منهما ينتج لا شيء من «ج أ». وكذلك في الموجبة كما إذا أخذ شيئان متباينان، وحمل جنس أحدهما على الآخر ثم حمل ذلك على المباين الآخر ينتج أن يحمل المباين على المباين كما إذا قلت كل إنسان حجر ، وكل حجر حيوان ينتج كل إنسان حيوان ولو قلت في الكبرى ولا شيء من الحجر بجماد ينتج لا شيء من الإنسان بجماد ولا يظن أنه ليس بقياس؛ والقياس هو قول مؤلف من أقوال متى سلمت لزم عنه لذاته قول آخر، وإنه كذلك.
Know that true premises entail a true conclusion; and it does not reverse, for the syllogism is like the antecedent to the consequent, and the conclusion is like the consequent, and it does not follow from the removal of the antecedent, which is the syllogism comprising the truth of the premises and the correctness of the composition, the removal of the consequent, which is the true conclusion; rather, from its positing follows the positing of the consequent, as is known. And since the case is this, it does not prevent that from false premises a true conclusion may follow, not because of their truth or the reality of those premises, but because it happens that the conclusion is true in itself, yet the syllogism composed of those false premises is arranged for its entailment. An example of that is when you say 'Every ج is ب, and No أ is ب' as two true premises, then from the second figure it produces 'No ج is أ'; then if you make the affirmative of them negative and the negative affirmative without changing the terms, and you say 'No ج is ب, and Every أ is ب', then each of them would be false at that time. Yet the composition from them produces 'No ج is أ'. And similarly in the affirmative: as when you take two disparate things and make the genus of one predicated of the other, then predicate that of the other disparate, it produces that the disparate is predicated of the disparate, as when you say 'Every human is a stone, and every stone is an animal', it produces 'Every human is an animal'; and if you say in the major 'No stone is inanimate', it produces 'No human is inanimate'. And do not think that it is not a syllogism; the syllogism is a statement composed of statements which, when conceded, entails by itself another statement, and it is so.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage explains the logical relationship between premises and conclusion, emphasizing that while true premises guarantee a true conclusion, the reverse is not required: a true conclusion can arise from false premises. The author opens by stating the principle: true premises entail a true conclusion, but the entailment is one-way. The syllogism is treated as an antecedent (muqaddam) and the conclusion as a consequent (tali); removing the antecedent does not remove the consequent, but positing the antecedent posits the consequent. Since the truth of the conclusion is not exclusively dependent on the truth of the premises, false premises can coincidentally yield a true conclusion. The author provides an example using the second figure with terms ج, ب, أ. When the premises 'Every ج is ب' and 'No أ is ب' are true, they entail 'No ج is أ'. If we swap the qualities to 'No ج is ب' and 'Every أ is ب', both become false, yet the syllogism still entails the same conclusion 'No ج is أ'. Another example with humans and stones shows that from false premises 'Every human is a stone' and 'Every stone is an animal', a true conclusion 'Every human is an animal' follows. The author clarifies that such arguments are genuine syllogisms as they satisfy the definition: a composed statement that entails another by itself. This passage is crucial for understanding the distinction between validity and soundness in Aristotelian logic as interpreted in the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
المقدمات الصادقة
النتيجة الصادقة
القياس
رفع المقدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, can a true conclusion follow from false premises in a valid syllogism?
A) No, a valid syllogism requires all premises to be true.
B) Yes, because validity concerns only form, not material truth.
C) Yes, but only if the false premises are accidentally true.
D) No, because false premises always produce false conclusions.

Key Takeaway
In a valid syllogism, true premises guarantee a true conclusion, but a true conclusion does not guarantee true premises; false premises can coincide with a true conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the following syllogism from kalam: 'The world is temporally originated (hadith); everything temporally originated requires a cause; therefore the world has a cause.' Identify whether the premises are true and whether the conclusion is true. Then determine if the syllogism is valid. According to the principle from this lesson, does the truth of the conclusion guarantee the truth of the premises? Explain.
